Manumission by a Safih Under Interdiction
Examines validity of manumission by a safih and the ensuing sa'yah obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on manumission by a safih under interdiction, evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument, and identify the sa'yah obligation according to the majority Hanafi view.

Core Idea
A safih's manumission is valid because it is too serious to be nullified by jest, unlike other transactions.

This passage presents a detailed inter-madhhab dispute regarding the validity of manumission (itq) by a safih (foolish spendthrift) who has been placed under financial interdiction (hajr). While the Shafi'i school treats the safih like a slave, rendering all transactions void except divorce, the Hanafi school—represented by the Sahibayn and Abu Hanifa—argues that manumission, being an act of grave religious significance, is unaffected by jest (hazl) and therefore remains valid despite interdiction. However, compensating the estate for the loss caused by this valid act introduces an internal Hanafi difference: whether the freed slave must earn his price through sa'yah.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that according to the Sahibayn (and Abu Hanifa), the manumission is valid and takes effect immediately.
This is the majority Hanafi view (zahir al-riwayah).
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
Note that Imam al-Shafi'i holds the manumission invalid, considering the safih akin to a slave whose transactions are void.
The disagreement is rooted in the fundamental analogy used.
3
Follow the Hanafi proof
Analyze the Hanafi reasoning: transactions affected by jest are blocked by hajr; but manumission is not affected by jest, so hajr does not block it. The safih is likened to a jester (hazil) who speaks irrationally but not due to mental deficiency.
Key principle: 'كل تصرف يؤثر فيه الهزل يؤثر فيه الحجر وما لا فلا'.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i proof
Understand the Shafi'i analogy: the safih under hajr is like a slave under slavery; both have restricted capacity, and only divorce is excepted. Thus manumission is void.
The Hanafi response: the safih owns property, the slave does not; hajr is for protection, not a right over his person.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and internal Hanafi difference
Conclude that in practice, the Hanafi view frees the slave but imposes sa'yah (earning his price) on the slave to compensate the estate, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Imam Muhammad denies sa'yah.
The majority (mufta bihi) is that manumission is valid and sa'yah is required.

Source Text

اس باب میں صاحبین کے یہاں اصول یہ ہے کہ جس تصرف میں ہنرل و تمسخر موثر ہو اس میں حجر بھی مؤثر ہوتا ہے اور جس میں ہنرل مؤثر نہ ہو اس میں حجر بھی مؤثر نہیں ہوتا۔ وجہ یہ ہے کہ سفیہ بھی ہازل کے معنی میں ہے بایں حیثیت کہ بازل کی گفتگو بوجہ خواہش نفس و
And if he manumits a slave, his manumission is effective according to the two (Sahibayn), while according to al-Shafi'i it is not effective. The principle according to the two is: every transaction in which jest (hazl) has effect, interdiction has effect in it; and whatever effect jest does not have, interdiction does not have effect. Because the safih is in the meaning of jester (hazil) insofar as the jester utters his speech not in the manner of rational people due to following desire and resisting intellect, not due to a deficiency in his intellect; likewise the safih. And manumission is something in which jest has no effect, so it is valid from him. The principle according to him (Shafi'i) is that interdiction due to folly is similar to interdiction due to slavery, such that after it none of his transactions take effect except divorce, like the slave. And manumission is not valid from a slave, so likewise from the safih. And when it is valid according to the two, the slave is required to earn (sa'yah) his value, because interdiction is for the meaning of consideration (nazar), and that lies in reversing the manumission, but it is impossible, so its reversal is obligatory by returning the value, as in interdiction over the sick. And from Muhammad it is narrated that earning (sa'yah) is not obligatory, because if it were obligatory, it would be a right for his manumitter, and the obligation of sa'yah in the Sharia is only known for the right of someone other than the manumitter. (Urdu translation and commentary follow explaining the same.)
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of manumission (itq) by a safih under interdiction (hajr). According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and also Abu Hanifa (as indicated by 'عندهما' including him), the manumission is effective (natif) and takes effect, unlike the view of Imam al-Shafi'i who considers it invalid. The reasoning of the Hanafis is based on a principle: any transaction that can be affected by jest (hazl) is also affected by interdiction; but manumission is too serious to be nullified by jest, so interdiction does not prevent it. Essentially, the safih is likened to a jester who speaks irrationally but not due to mental deficiency; since a jester's manumission would be valid, so is the safih's. In contrast, Imam al-Shafi'i analogizes the safih to a slave: a slave's manumission is invalid because he himself is property, and the safih's capacity is similarly restricted, so his manumission is void. The Hanafis, however, hold that the safih is the owner of the slave, and the hajr only restricts transactions for his own protection, not because of a property right over him. After the manumission is valid, the text addresses the financial consequence: the freed slave must work to pay his price (sa'yah) according to the majority of Hanafis, to compensate for the loss caused to the estate. This is similar to the case of a sick person who manumits a slave from his sickbed (marad al-mawt) where the slave has to pay his value if it exceeds one-third. However, there is a transmission from Imam Muhammad denying the obligation of sa'yah, arguing that sa'yah in Sharia is only imposed to benefit a creditor other than the manumitter, not to benefit the manumitter himself.

Key Terms
هزل (hazl): Jest or joking, legally significant because certain serious acts (like divorce, manumission) are not nullified by jest in Hanafi law.
سعاية (sa'yah): The obligation of a freed slave to earn money to pay his own price (value) to the owner's estate when the manumission was made under circumstances that harm creditors or estate.
عتق (itq): Manumission, freeing a slave, considered a highly meritorious act that is effective even from a safih according to Hanafis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view (Sahibayn), if a safih under interdiction manumits a slave, what is the consequence regarding the slave?
A) The manumission is invalid; the slave remains a slave.
B) The manumission is valid but the slave must pay his value through sa'yah.
C) The manumission is valid and the slave is free with no further obligation.
D) The manumission is suspended pending the qadi's approval.

Key Takeaway
Manumission by a safih under interdiction is valid in Hanafi law, but the freed slave must earn his price to compensate the estate, according to the majority.

Manumission by an Interdicted Spendthrift: The Hazal Principle
Inter-madhhab dispute on manumission by a safih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on manumission by a safih, explain the Sahibayn's principle of hazal, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-analogy.

Core Idea
Manumission is not affected by jest, so a safih's manumission stands, but the slave must earn his value.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a key dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the validity of manumission by a person interdicted due to spendthrift (safah). The Hanafi school holds that manumission is valid because it is not affected by jest (hazal), whereas the Shafi'i school analogizes the safih to a slave. Understanding this dispute illuminates how the concept of legal capacity (ahliyyah) and the effect of interdiction (hajr) are applied differently across madhhabs.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and also Abu Hanifa), manumission by a safih is valid; the slave is freed but must earn his value (sa'y) as compensation.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that manumission is invalid, based on analogy to a slave under interdiction.
3
Follow the Hanafi proof (the hazal principle)
The Hanafi principle: any disposition affected by hazal (jest) is affected by hajr (interdiction); what is not affected by hazal is not affected by hajr. Since manumission is not affected by hazal (a joking manumission is valid in Hanafi law), interdiction does not prevent its validity.
The safih is likened to a jester who acts irrationally but has full intellect; his testaments are not void due to irrationality alone.
4
Analyse the Shafi'i proof
Al-Shafi'i analogizes the safih to a slave: just as a slave's manumission is invalid because he lacks legal capacity, so is a safih's manumission invalid because his interdiction restricts all dispositions except divorce.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: In the Hanafi school, the slave is freed but must pay his value; in the Shafi'i school, the manumission is void and the slave remains a slave.
The Hanafi response refutes the analogy to slavery: the reason for interdiction differs – slavery denies all legal capacity, safah only restricts financial transactions for protection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان اعتق عبدا نفذ عتقه عندهما وعند الشافعى لا ينفذ والاصل عندهما ان كل تصرف يؤثر فيه الهزل يؤثر فيه الحجر وما لا فلا لان السفيه فى معنى الهازل من حيث ان الهازل يخرج كلامه لا على نهج كلام العقلاء لاتباع الهوى ومكابرة العقل لا لنقصان في عقله فكذلك السفيه والعتق مما لا يؤثر فيه الهزل فيصح منه والاصل عنده ان الحجر بسبب السفه بمنزلة الحجر بسبب الرق حتى لا ينفذ بعده شيء من تصرفاته الا الطلاق كالمرقوق والاعتاق لا يصح من الرقيق فكذلك من السفيه واذا صح عندهما كان على العبد ان يسعى في قيمته لان الحجر لمعنى النظر وذلك في ردّ العتق الا انه متعذر فيجب رده برد القيمة كما في الحجر على المريض وعن محمد انه لا يجب السعاية لانها لو وجبت انما تجب حقا لمعتقه والسعاية ما . عُهد وجوبها فى الشرع الا لحق غير المعتق
If he manumits a slave, his manumission is effective according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and according to al-Shafi'i it is not effective. Their principle is that every disposition in which jest is effective, interdiction is effective in it, and what not, then not because the safih is in the meaning of the jester, in that the jester utters his speech not in the manner of rational people due to following desire and opposing reason, not due to a defect in his intellect, so also the safih. And manumission is among those things in which jest is not effective, so it is valid from him. And his (al-Shafi'i's) principle is that interdiction due to safah is like interdiction due to slavery such that no disposition of his is effective after it except divorce, like the slave, and manumission is not valid from the slave, so also from the safih. And when it is valid according to them, the slave must earn his value because the interdiction is for the purpose of consideration, and that is in repudiating the manumission, but it is impossible, so it must be repudiated by repudiating the value, as in interdiction over the sick. And from Muhammad that earning is not obligatory because if it were obligatory, it would be obligatory as a right for his manumitter, and obligation of earning is not known in the Shari'ah except for the right of other than the manumitter.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of manumission by a safih (spendthrift) after he has been placed under legal interdiction (hajr). According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and also Abu Hanifa according to the commentary), the manumission is effective and the slave is freed, but the slave must earn (sa'y) his value as compensation to protect the safih's interests. Imam Shafi'i holds that the manumission is invalid. The Sahibayn base their ruling on a principle: any legal act that can be affected by jest (hazal) is also affected by interdiction; since manumission is not affected by jest (one cannot joke about freeing a slave in a binding manner), interdiction does not prevent its validity. They analogize the safih to a jester in that both act without full rationality but not due to insanity. Imam Shafi'i analogizes the safih to a slave under interdiction (mamluk), where all dispositions except divorce are invalid. The commentary notes that Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani) has a separate opinion that the slave is not required to earn his value, because the right to earn is only established for someone other than the manumitter, as in the case of a co-owner freeing a slave.

Key Terms
سفيه (safīh): A legally impudent spendthrift who is interdicted due to foolishness (safah) in financial matters.
حجر (hajr): Legal interdiction preventing a person from disposing of his property to protect his interest.
هزل (hazal): Jest or joking; legally, jokes that affect the seriousness of an act are considered.
سعاية (sa‘āyah): Earning by a slave to compensate for his value when freed without proper authorization.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why is a safih's manumission valid?
A) Because the safih is like a slave whose manumission is valid.
B) Because manumission is not affected by jest, and the safih is analogous to a jester.
C) Because the safih has regained rationality after interdiction.
D) Because the judge's permission was obtained.

Key Takeaway
A safih's manumission is valid because manumission is not affected by hazal; the slave must earn his value to protect the safih's interest.

Making a Mudabbar by an Interdicted Spendthrift
Ruling on tadbir by a interdicted spendthrift with qiyas to manumission and umm walad claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tadbir by a safih, identify when the slave must earn his value (sa'y) upon the master's death, and apply the same principle to claims of paternity of a slave-girl's child.

Core Idea
A safih's tadbir is valid; the slave must earn value at death to protect heirs.

This lesson examines a specific scenario where a spendthrift (safih) under legal interdiction (hajr) makes his slave a mudabbar—a slave promised freedom upon the master's death. The Hanafi school balances the validity of manumission rights with the protection of creditors and heirs. The key distinction is that the slave does not have to earn his value during the master's lifetime, but must do so upon the master's death if the master never regains legal capacity. The passage also extends this reasoning to claims of paternity and umm walad status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
State the act: a safih (interdicted spendthrift) makes his slave a mudabbar. The ruling: it is permissible (ja'iz).
This is the first sentence of the passage.
2
Understand the reasoning (qiyas)
Tadbir creates a right of manumission (haqq al-'itq), similar to immediate manumission, which is valid despite interdiction. Both are effective because manumission is not nullified by hazal (spendthrift conduct).
The text says 'li-annahu yujibu haqq al-'itq'.
3
Analyze the timing of sa'y
The slave does not have to earn his value (sa'y) during the master's life because he remains in the master's ownership. But upon the master's death (if he died without rushd), the slave must earn his value as a mudabbar because he becomes free at death and the tadbir is a gratuitous disposition affecting the estate.
Sa'y is deferred to protect heirs/creditors.
4
Apply the analogy to umm walad cases
If the safih claims paternity of a slave-girl's child, the child is free and she becomes umm walad. If no child, declaring her umm walad still makes her unsellable, and at death she must earn her full value (no child witness). This parallels the sick person's case.
The child witnesses for her in the first case, so no sa'y needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو دبّر عبده جاز لانه يوجب حق العتق فيعتبر بحقيقته الا انه لا يجب السعاية مادام المولى حيا لانه باق على ملكه واذا مات ولم يؤنس منه الرشد سعى فى قيمته مدبرا لانه عتق بموته وهو مدبر فصار كما اذا اعتقه بعد التدبير ولو جاءت جاريته بولد فادعاه يثبت نسبه منه وكان الولد حرَّا والجارية ام ولد له لانه محتاج الى ذلك لابقاء نسله فالحق بالمصلح فى حقه وان لم يكن معها ولد وقال هذه ام ولدى كانت بمنزلة ام الولد لا يقدر على بيعها وان مات سعت في جميع قيمتها لانه كالاقرار بالحرية اذ ليس لها شهادة الولد بخلاف الفصل الاول لان الولد شاهد لها ونظيره المريض اذا ادعى ولد جاريته فهو على هذا التفصيل
And if he makes his slave a mudabbar, it is permissible because it creates a right of manumission, so it is considered according to its reality. However, earning is not obligatory as long as the master is alive because the slave remains in his ownership. And if he dies and no sign of maturity (rushd) is perceived from him, then the slave earns his value as a mudabbar, because he is freed by his death while he is a mudabbar, so it is like the case where he manumits him after tadbir. And if his slave-girl comes with a child and he claims it, his lineage from him is established, the child is free, and the slave-girl becomes umm walad for him because he needs that for the continuance of his progeny, so he is attached to the one who acts properly in his right. And if there is no child with her and he says 'This is my umm walad,' she is like an umm walad, he cannot sell her, and if he dies she earns her entire value because it is like an acknowledgment of freedom since she has no witness of a child, unlike the first case because the child witnesses for her. And its parallel is the sick person if he claims a child of his slave-girl; it is according to this distinction.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage rules on the validity of a safih (interdicted spendthrift) making his slave a mudabbar (a slave who is to be freed upon the master's death). According to the Sahibayn and Abu Hanifa, this is permissible because tadbir establishes a right of manumission, similar to immediate manumission which is valid. However, the slave does not have to earn his value (sa'y) during the master's lifetime because he remains in the master's ownership. If the safih dies without regaining legal capacity (rushd), then upon his death the mudabbar becomes free, and the slave must earn his value for the sake of the safih's heirs (or creditors) because the tadbir is a gratuitous disposition that reduces the estate. The commentary then discusses a related issue: if the safih claims paternity of a child born to his slave-girl, the child is free and the slave-girl becomes umm walad (mother of the master's child), because this is necessary for preserving his lineage. If there is no child and he merely declares her umm walad, she cannot be sold, and upon his death she must earn her full value. This is analogized to the case of a sick person making such a claim.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who is promised freedom upon the master's death, contingent on the master's will.
ام ولد (umm walad): A female slave who bears a child to her master; she becomes free upon the master's death.
سعاية (sa‘āyah): The requirement for the slave to earn his value to compensate for a disposition that exceeds the master's disposal rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When must a mudabbar slave of a safih earn his value?
A) Immediately upon the tadbir.
B) When the safih dies without regaining rushd.
C) When the safih regains rushd.
D) The slave never earns value.

Key Takeaway
A safih's tadbir is valid, but the slave must earn his value upon the master's death if the master dies without regaining legal capacity.

Validity of Tadbir by a Safih and Sa'y Upon Death
Ruling on safih's tadbir with commentary expansion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a safih's tadbir is valid and explain why sa'y is only required after the master's death.

Core Idea
A safih's tadbir is valid; sa'y is only required after death, not during lifetime.

Can a person under interdiction (safih) declare that his slave will be free upon his death? The Hanafi school says yes. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind that ruling, the timing of the slave's obligation to work for his value (sa'y), and how it differs from other forms of manumission. We also explore the case of a female slave and the effect of claiming paternity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is tadbir (declaration of freedom upon death) by a safih. The ruling: it is valid (sahih).
2
Analyze the proof: qiyas on real rights
The author analogizes tadbir to a real right (haqiqi haqq) because the safih can initiate real rights, so by priority he can initiate a right like tadbir.
This is a qiyas jaliy argument.
3
Understand the condition: sa'y not during lifetime
While the master is alive, the slave remains in his ownership, so imposing sa'y (working to pay value) is impossible because a master cannot be debited to his own slave.
This contrasts with kitabat or freeing for compensation, where the obligation arises immediately.
4
Apply to female slave cases
If the safih's female slave bears a child and he claims paternity, the child is free and she becomes umm walad with no sa'y. If no child, her mere claim as umm walad makes her like umm walad but she must perform sa'y upon his death.
Analogy: the child is a witness in the first case; without a child, her status requires sa'y.

تشریح قوله وان دبر الخ۔ اگر سفیہ نے اپنا غلام مدبر کیا تو جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ تدبیر موجب حق عشق ہے تو اس کو حقیقی حق پر قیاس کیا جائے گا۔ اسلئے کہ جب وہ حقیقت عشق کے انشاء کا مالک ہے تو حق عشق کے انشاء کا بطریق اولی مالک ہوگا۔ لیکن اس صورت میں جب تک آقا زندہ ہے غلام پر سعایت واجب نہ ہوگی۔ کیونکہ وہ ابھی تک اس کی ملک پر باقی ہے تو ایجاب سعایت ناممکن ہے۔ کیونکہ آقا اپنے غلام پر مستوجب دین نہیں ہوتا۔ چنانچہ اگر اس نے غلام کو بعوض مال مدبر کیا اور غلام نے قبول بھی کر لیا نو تدبیر صحیح ہوتی ہے اور مال واجب نہیں ہوتا اس کے برخلاف اگر مکاتب کیا یا بعوض مال آزاد کیا تو ہے۔ لانہ لم یبق علی ملکہ حقیقت اویدا۔ قوله واذا مات .... الخ - اگر سفیہ مذکور اس حال میں مرگیا کہ ہنوز اس سے آثار رشد ظاہر نہیں ہوئے تھے ۔ تو غلام مذکورمد بر - ہونے کے حساب سے اپنی قیمت میں سعایت کرے گا۔ کیونکہ وہ آقا کے مرنے پر اس حال میں آزاد ہوا کہ وہ مدبر تھا تو ایسا ہو گیا جیسے اس نے مد بر کرنے کے بعد آزاد کیا ہو۔
The [slave] will work for his price according to his status as mudabbar, because he becomes free upon the master's death. In this case, since he is male, it is as if he was freed after tadbir. If the safih's female slave has a child and the safih claims the child's lineage, then the lineage is established, the child is free, and the female slave becomes umm walad, because he needs her for preserving his progeny, so she is treated in the right of progeny with benefit. If the female slave has no child and the safih says 'She is my umm walad', then she becomes like an umm walad such that he cannot sell her, and if he dies, she will work for her full price, because this is in the rank of acknowledgment of freedom, because for the female slave there is no testimony of a child, unlike the first case where the child is her witness. And its analogy is the sick person when he claims his female slave's child; this is also on the same detail. Commentary: His statement 'And if he makes tadbir...' If a safih makes his slave mudabbar, it is permissible, because tadbir is a cause of the right of the like of 'ishq, so it is analogized to a real right. Because when he owns the initiation of real 'ishq, then by primary analogy he owns the initiation of the right of 'ishq. But in this case, as long as the master is alive, sa'y is not obligatory on the slave, because he still remains in his ownership, so imposing sa'y is impossible, because the master is not indebted to his slave. Thus, if he made the slave mudabbar in exchange for property and the slave accepted, then tadbir is valid and the property is not obligatory, unlike if he made him mukatab or freed him in exchange for property. His statement 'And when he dies...' If the aforementioned safih dies while signs of prudence have not appeared from him, then the aforementioned slave will work for his price according to his status as mudabbar, because he became free upon the master's death in a state that he was mudabbar, so it becomes as if he freed him after making him mudabbar.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on tadbir (manumission by will) by a safih (a foolish person under interdiction). The author first presents the ruling that if a safih makes his slave mudabbar, it is valid, and upon the safih's death, the slave becomes free and must work for his value (sa'y). The reasoning is that tadbir is a form of ownership right, and since the safih can initiate real rights, he can initiate this by priority. However, during the safih's lifetime, the slave does not have to work because he remains in ownership and a master is not obligated to pay a debt to his slave. The text then contrasts with kitabat (contract of manumission) or freeing for compensation, where the compensation becomes obligatory. The passage also discusses the case of a female slave: if she bears a child and the safih claims paternity, the child is free and she becomes umm walad (mother of a child), and she cannot be sold. If there is no child, the safih's mere claim makes her like umm walad but she must still work for her price upon his death, because there is no child to testify for her. This is analogized to a sick person's claim. The commentary expands by comparing tadbir to real rights and explains the legal reasoning (qiyas). The authority weight is from the Hanafi school's Zahir al-Riwayah, as this is from Al-Hidayah commentary.

Key Terms
تدبير (tadbir): A declaration by a master that his slave will be free upon the master's death.
سعایت (sa'y): The slave's effort to earn money to pay his value to the master's heirs upon freedom.
ام ولد (umm walad): A female slave who bears a child from her master; she becomes free upon the master's death and cannot be sold.

Key Takeaway
A safih's tadbir is valid and the slave's sa'y is only triggered after the master's death, not during his lifetime.

Dispute on Interdiction for Debt: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on interdiction due to debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on interdiction for debt and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Imprisonment suffices; interdiction nullifies capacity — Abu Hanifa prohibits hajr for debt, Sahibayn permit it to protect creditors.

This lesson explores a key intra-Hanafi dispute: whether a judge can impose interdiction (hajr) on a debtor to protect creditors. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that interdiction nullifies legal capacity and is impermissible, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allow it by analogy to interdiction of the foolish (safih). The chapter in Al-Hidayah follows the Sahibayn's view, indicating the mufta bihi position. Understanding this dispute sharpens one's ability to distinguish between imprisonment and interdiction in Hanafi commercial law.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position
Identify the hukm: Abu Hanifa does not impose interdiction for debt. He relies on imprisonment until the debtor sells property voluntarily.
The key is that interdiction nullifies ahliyyah (legal capacity), which is a severe measure.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow interdiction if creditors request it. The judge prevents the debtor from selling below fair price or making harmful transactions.
This protects creditors from collusive sales (talji'ah).
3
Follow the Sahibayn's proof
They use qiyas: just as interdiction is allowed for a safih (foolish person) for his own benefit, here it is for the benefit of creditors.
The illa (effective cause) is preventing harm; in safih it is harm to self, here harm to others.
4
Analyse Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa argues that nullifying capacity is not permissible to prevent a specific harm. Imprisonment is sufficient to compel payment without stripping the debtor's capacity.
The judge cannot dispose of the debtor's property without consent.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the debtor remains free to transact but may be imprisoned. Under Sahibayn, the debtor is interdicted and cannot sell or deal without permission, except at fair price.
The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view, as implied by the chapter title.

Chapter of interdiction due to debt. Translation: Chapter on compelling due to debt. The position of Imam Abu Hanifa regarding not imposing interdiction due to debt. Abu Hanifa said: I do not impose interdiction due to debt. When debts become due on a man and creditors request his imprisonment and interdiction, I do not impose interdiction on him, because in interdiction there is nullification of his legal capacity, so it is not permissible to repel a specific harm. If he has wealth, the judge does not dispose of it, because that is a type of interdiction and because it is a transaction without mutual consent, so it is void by text. But he imprisons him forever until he sells his property to pay his debt, fulfilling the creditors' right and repelling his injustice. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: When creditors of an insolvent person request interdiction, the judge imposes interdiction on him and prevents him from selling, transacting, and confessing, so that he does not harm the creditors, because we permitted interdiction on the foolish (safih) for his own benefit, and in this interdiction there is benefit for the creditors, since he may dispose of his property in a collusive manner (talji'ah) and their right may be lost. The meaning of their statement 'prevent him from selling' is that the sale is below the fair price; as for selling at the fair price, it does not nullify the creditors' right, and the prevention is for their right, so he is not prevented from that. Commentary: The title of this chapter is established as 'Chapter of interdiction due to debt', and the previous chapter was titled 'Chapter of interdiction due to foolishness', which is based on the view of the two. As in the Book of Prayer, the section of Takbirat al-Tashriq and in the knowledge of fara'id, the chapter of division with grandfather, similarly it is said: because Abu Hanifa does not see any of these. Then the interdiction that occurs due to debt, because it is dependent on the creditors, includes a description of participation in the original interdiction, so it is like the relation of a compound to a simple, hence this chapter is delayed after the interdiction of the foolish.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding interdiction (hajr) for debt. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that a judge should not impose interdiction on a debtor, even if creditors request it, because interdiction nullifies the person's legal capacity (ahliyyah) and this is not permissible to prevent a specific harm. The debtor's property cannot be disposed of by the judge without consent. Instead, the debtor is imprisoned until he sells his property to pay off his debts. This preserves the creditors' rights while avoiding the nullification of the debtor's capacity. The two (Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) hold that if creditors of an insolvent person (muflis) request interdiction, the judge should impose it, preventing the debtor from selling, transacting, or making confessions that could harm the creditors. Their reasoning is that just as interdiction is allowed for a foolish person (safih) for his own benefit, here it is allowed for the benefit of the creditors, because the debtor might otherwise dispose of his property in a collusive manner (talji'ah) to evade payment. However, the Sahibayn clarify that the debtor is only prevented from selling below the fair price (thaman al-mithl); selling at the fair price is permitted because it does not harm the creditors. The commentary notes that the chapter title 'Bab al-Hajr bi Sabab al-Dayn' is based on the view of the Sahibayn, indicating that their position is the one followed in practice.

Key Terms
حجر (hajr): Interdiction: a legal restriction preventing a person from disposing of their property, usually due to foolishness, insanity, or debt.
مفلس (muflis): An insolvent person whose debts exceed his assets.
غرماء (ghurama'): Creditors who are owed debts by the muflis.
سفيه (safih): A foolish or prodigal person who mismanages wealth, subject to interdiction for his own benefit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the Sahibayn, at what price is a debtor allowed to sell his property even after interdiction?
A) Below the market price
B) At the market price (thaman al-mithl)
C) Any price
D) Only with the judge's permission

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that a debtor may be interdicted by the judge at the creditors' request to prevent collusive sales, while imprisonment is the alternative in Abu Hanifa's view.

Legal Interdiction for Debt: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on hajr for debt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on legal interdiction for debt and explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, including the practical difference in rulings.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa forbids interdiction for debt; the Sahibayn allow it to protect creditors.

In Hanafi fiqh, legal interdiction (hajr) restricts a person's disposal of property. A major dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Sahibayn) concerns whether a debtor can be interdicted for the benefit of creditors. This lesson unpacks their arguments, proofs, and the consequences for practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The dispute is about whether a judge can impose interdiction on an insolvent debtor (muflis) at the request of creditors, preventing him from selling or transacting.
The issue is not about imprisonment but restricting legal capacity.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and dalil
Abu Hanifa rejects interdiction because it nullifies ahliyyah (legal capacity). His primary proof is Quran 4:29, which prohibits trade without mutual consent.
He allows imprisonment but not forced sale.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and dalil
The Sahibayn permit interdiction by analogy (qiyas) to interdiction on a safih (foolish person). Both aim to prevent harm: to the safih himself, to creditors here.
They limit interdiction to sales below fair value.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response to Sahibayn
He distinguishes: interdiction on safih is for his own benefit (protection), while for debt it is for others — not enough to nullify capacity. The Quranic text takes priority over qiyas.
Thus Abu Hanifa maintains his position despite analogy.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
Later Hanafi works (Ikhtiyar, Durr al-Mukhtar) give fatwa on the Sahibayn's view. The practical consequence is that judges can interdiction debtors to protect creditors.
This is the operative ruling in most Hanafi jurisdictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحجر
باب الحجر بسبب الدين
قال ابو حنيفة لا احجر فى الدين واذا وجبت ديون على رجل وطلب غرماء حبسه والحجر عليه لم احجر عليه لان فى الحجر اهدار اهليته فلا يجوز لدفع ضرر خاص فان كان له مال لم يتصرف فيه الحاكم لانه نوع حجر ولانه تجارة لا عن تراض فيكون باطلا بالنص ولكن يحبسه ابدا حتى يبيعه في دينه ايفاء لحق الغرماء ودفعا لظلمه وقالا اذا طلب غرماء المفلس الحجر عليه حجر القاضي عليه ومنعه من البيع والتصرف والاقرار حتى لا يضر Chapter of Legal Interdiction. Chapter of Legal Interdiction due to Debt. Abu Hanifa said: I do not impose interdiction for debt. When debts are owed by a man and creditors ask for his imprisonment and interdiction, I do not impose interdiction because in interdiction there is nullification of his legal capacity, so it is not permissible to ward off a specific harm. If he has property, the judge shall not dispose of it because it is a type of interdiction, and because it is trade without mutual consent, so it is void by the text. But he shall imprison him forever until he sells his property to pay his debt, fulfilling the right of the creditors and repelling his oppression. And the two (Sahibayn) said: When the creditors of the insolvent request interdiction upon him, the judge imposes interdiction upon him and prevents him from selling, transacting, and confessing, so that he does not harm the creditors. Because we have permitted interdiction on the foolish person for his own benefit, and in this interdiction there is benefit for the creditors, because perhaps he will dispose of his property in a way that defeats their right. The meaning of their statement 'prevent him from selling' is that it should be for less than the fair price. As for selling at the fair price, it does not invalidate the right of the creditors, and prohibition is due to their right, so he is not prevented from it. Translation: Imam Abu Hanifa says...
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding legal interdiction (hajr) due to debt. The text first states the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa: he does not impose hajr on a debtor even if creditors request it. His reasoning is that hajr nullifies legal capacity (ahliyyah) and thus cannot be used to ward off a specific harm (darar khas) like debt. Instead, the judge can imprison the debtor until he sells his own property to pay the debt. If the debtor has property, the judge cannot sell it without the debtor's consent because that would be a trade without mutual consent, which is explicitly prohibited in the Quran (4:29). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ: they allow the judge to impose hajr upon the request of creditors, preventing the insolvent from selling, transacting, or confessing to protect creditors' rights. They analogize to hajr on a foolish person (safih), which is for his own benefit; here it is for the creditors' benefit. The commentary clarifies that the Sahibayn only prevent selling for less than fair market value; selling at fair value is allowed. The passage ends by acknowledging that fatwa is given on the opinion of the Sahibayn in the later texts.

Key Terms
حجر (hajr): Legal interdiction preventing a person from disposing of his property due to a legal incapacity.
مفلس (muflis): An insolvent debtor whose debts exceed his assets.
تلجیہ (talji'ah): A disposition of property intended to evade creditors by selling at an undervalue or in a fraudulent manner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, what is the judge's authority regarding an insolvent debtor who wishes to sell his property below fair market value?
A) The judge can interdiction the debtor and prevent the sale.
B) The judge cannot interdiction the debtor because interdiction is not allowed for debt.
C) The judge can only imprison the debtor and wait for him to sell.
D) The judge can sell the property himself without the debtor's consent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on interdiction for debt: Abu Hanifa rejects it, but the Sahibayn allow it, and their view is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
9 min
Qadi's Authority to Sell Muflis's Property: Abu Hanifah vs. Sahibayn
Ruling on qadi selling muflis's property when he refuses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions regarding the qadi's authority to sell the property of a muflis who refuses, and evaluate the arguments of Imam Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The obligation is to repay the debt, not necessarily by selling; forced sale is an unjustified infringement.

When a debtor (muflis) refuses to sell his property to pay creditors, does the qadi have the authority to sell it for him? This passage presents an important intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifah holds that the qadi cannot force a sale, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that he can, analogizing to the qadi's role in divorce cases. Understanding the reasoning behind each position—especially the distinction between obligation of means and obligation of result—is key to applying the correct ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is the qadi selling the muflis's property without his consent. The dispute is whether this is permissible.
2
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
The Sahibayn say the qadi can sell because selling is obligatory on the debtor; if he refuses, the qadi substitutes like in cases of impotence (majbub) or aversion (an-nin).
Their analogy is from family law: qadi can pronounce divorce when husband refuses.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifah's first response (talji'ah objection)
Abu Hanifah notes that fear of a collusive sale (talji'ah) is hypothetical and cannot strip the debtor of legal capacity.
Talji'ah is a fake sale to avoid creditors; its mere possibility doesn't justify forced sale.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifah's second response (means vs. end)
Abu Hanifah argues that the obligation is to repay the debt, not specifically to sell. Other means exist (e.g., borrowing, gifts, or imprisonment leading to payment). Unlike divorce, where the qadi's substitution is the only way, here it is not exclusive.
Imprisonment is not to force sale but to induce repayment by any chosen means.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
If the qadi can sell, the debtor's property is sold immediately. If not, the debtor is imprisoned until he pays otherwise, preserving his right to choose the method.
This affects the debtor's autonomy and the creditor's speed of recovery.

Source Text

تشریح .. قوله وباع ماله.... الخ - اگر مفلس اپنا مال بیچنے سے باز رہے تو صاحبین کے نزدیک قاضی اس کو فروخت کر کے ثمن قرض خواہوں کو حصہ رسد تقسیم کر دے۔ کیونکہ قرض چکانے کیلئے مال فروخت کرنا مدیون پر واجب ہے۔ یہاں تک کہ وہ اس کی وجہ سے قید کیا جاتا ہے پس اس کے باز رہنے پر قاضی اس کا قائم مقام ہو گیا۔ جیسے محبوب و عنین اگر بیوی کو طلاق دینے سے باز رہے تو قاضی اس کا قائم مقام ہو کر طلاق دے دیتا ہے۔

If the muflis refrains from selling his property, the qadi should sell and divide the proceeds among his creditors in proportion, according to them (the Sahibayn), because selling is obligatory upon him to repay his debt, so much so that he is imprisoned for it. When he refrains, the qadi stands in his place, as in the case of the impotent (majbub) and the one who is averse (an-nin). We say: the talji'ah is merely hypothetical. What is required is the repayment of the debt, and selling is not the exclusive means for that, unlike the case of the impotent and the one who is averse. Imprisonment for debt repayment is so that he may choose whatever means he wishes. How can it be that if selling is valid, imprisonment would be harmful to both parties—delaying the creditor's right and causing suffering to the debtor—so it would not be legislated.
Commentary on his statement: 'and he sells his property...' If the muflis refrains, according to the Sahibayn the qadi sells and distributes the price proportionally. Because selling is obligatory upon the debtor to repay his debt, even to the point of imprisonment. When he refrains, the qadi replaces him, as in the case of the impotent husband who refrains from divorcing, the qadi divorces on his behalf. Commentary on his statement: 'we say: the talji'ah...' This is a response to the Sahibayn's statement 'because perhaps he will resort to talji'ah'. The response is that this is merely hypothetical—that the debtor might sell his property as a talji'ah. So a hypothetical matter cannot strip a person of legal capacity. And the response to 'because selling is obligatory upon him' is that the debtor is only obliged to repay his debt, and selling is not the exclusive means for that; there are other means, e.g., he can be imprisoned and then sell, or he can obtain money through loan, gift, or begging. Unlike the impotent and the one who is averse, because when he cannot retain with kindness, then releasing with goodness becomes the exclusive means; there is no other way except the qadi's substitution. Commentary on his statement: 'and imprisonment...' This is a response to the Sahibayn's statement 'so much so that he is imprisoned for it'. The response is that imprisonment is admitted, but the purpose of imprisonment is not to force a sale; rather, it is to induce him to choose any means of repayment. If selling were permissible, then imprisoning the debtor would harm both: the creditor's right is delayed and the debtor suffers needlessly, so it would not be legislated.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute between Imam Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the qadi's authority to sell the property of an insolvent debtor who refuses to sell. According to the Sahibayn, if the muflis refuses, the qadi may sell his property and distribute the proceeds to creditors, analogizing to the qadi's authority in cases of impotence (jubb) or aversion (unnah) where the qadi can pronounce divorce. Their proof is that selling is obligatory, and if the debtor refuses, the qadi stands in his place. Imam Abu Hanifah disagrees, arguing that the fear of talji'ah (a collusive sale) is hypothetical and cannot strip the debtor of capacity. Moreover, the obligation is to repay the debt, and selling is not the only means; imprisonment can induce the debtor to repay through other means. Furthermore, if the qadi could force a sale, imprisonment would become harmful—delaying the creditor's right and causing useless suffering to the debtor—thus it would not be legislated. The Hanafi position is that the qadi cannot force a sale; the debtor may be imprisoned until he pays or arranges payment by any means.

Key Terms
مجبوب (majbub): A man who is impotent (literally: castrated). In fiqh, if he cannot consummate marriage, the wife may seek annulment and the qadi may separate them.
عنين (un-nin): A man who is averse to marital relations; if he cannot fulfill marital duties, the qadi may separate them.
تلجية (talji'ah): A collusive sale to avoid creditors; the sale is valid in form but intended to defeat creditors' rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifah, why can the qadi not force the sale of the muflis's property?
A) Because the debtor has the right to choose any means of repayment
B) Because selling is not obligatory
C) Because the Sahibayn forbid it
D) Because the debtor is not insolvent

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school (Abu Hanifah) holds that a qadi cannot force-sell a muflis's property; imprisonment is used to compel repayment by any means, preserving the debtor's autonomy.

The Qadi's Authority to Discharge Debt: Consensus and Istihsan
Ruling on qadi paying debt with same currency and istihsan for different currency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the qadi paying debt with the same currency and explain the istihsan that allows selling different currency, including the basis in thumaniyyah and maliyyah.

Core Idea
The qadi may sell different currency by istihsan: shared monetary value gives authority, difference in form restricts the creditor.

This lesson examines the qadi's (judge's) authority to discharge debts when the debtor and creditor are in different positions. We begin with a clear consensus: when both debt and asset are dirhams, the qadi pays directly. Then we explore a more nuanced scenario—debt in dirhams, assets in dinars—where Abu Hanifah applies istihsan to allow sale, overruling the qiyas that would prohibit it. The reasoning hinges on the dual nature of currency: shared monetary essence vs. specific form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish the case where debt and asset are same currency (both dirhams) from the case where they differ (debt dirhams, asset dinars, or vice versa).
The first is by consensus; the second is by istihsan.
2
Understand the consensus for same currency
When debt and asset are both dirhams, the qadi may pay the creditor without the debtor's permission, because the creditor already has the right to take without consent.
This is agreed upon by all scholars.
3
Recognize the qiyas prohibition for different currency
By analogy with commodities (urud), the qadi should not be allowed to sell dinars to pay a dirham debt, because forced sale of specific property is not permitted.
This is the 'qiyas' side.
4
Grasp the istihsan reasoning
Despite formal difference, dirhams and dinars share the attribute of being mediums of exchange (thumaniyyah) and monetary value (maliyyah). This unity gives the qadi authority to dispose of them, while the difference prevents the creditor from forcible seizure.
This dual consideration justifies the sale.
5
Note the order of sale and debtor's clothing
The qadi sells currency first, then commodities, then real estate, starting with the easiest. The debtor is left with one or two sets of clothing as a minimum.
This balances expediting repayment with the debtor's dignity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مدیون کا قرض دراہم ہوں اور مال بھی دراہم تو قاضی بغیر اجازت کے قرض چکا دے
قال وان كان دينه دراهم و له دراهم قضى القاضى بغير امره وهذا بالاجماع لان للدائن حق الاخذ من غير رضاه فللقاضى ان يعينه وان كان دينه دراهم وله دنانير او على ضد ذلك باعها القاضي في دينه وهذا عند ابي حنيفة استحسان والقياس ان لا يبيعه كما فى العروض ولهذا لم يكن لصاحب الدين ان ياخذه جبرا وجه الاستحسان انهما متحدان فى الثمنية والمالية مختلفان فى الصورة فبالنظر الى الاتحاد يثبت للقاضي ولاية التصرف وبالنظر الى الاختلاف يُسلب عن الدائن ولاية الاخذ عملا بالشبهين بخلاف العروض لان الغرض يتعلق بصورها واعيانها اما النقود فوسائل فافترقا ويباع فى الدين النقود ثم العروض ثم العقار يُبدأ بالايسر فالايسر لما فيه من المسارعة الى قضاء الدين مع مراعاة جانب المديون ويُترك عليه دست من ثياب بدنه ويباع الباقى لان به كفاية وقيل دستان لانه اذا غسل ثيابه لابد له من ملبس
If the debtor's debt is dirhams and he has dirhams, the qadi pays without his permission, and this is by consensus, because the creditor has the right to take without consent, so the qadi may specify it. If his debt is dirhams and he has dinars, or the opposite, the qadi sells them for his debt. This, according to Abu Hanifah, is istihsan. The qiyas is that he should not sell them, as in the case of commodities. For this reason, the creditor does not have the right to take them forcibly. The face of istihsan is that they (dirhams and dinars) are united in being mediums of exchange (thumaniyyah) and monetary value (maliyyah) but differ in form. Considering the unity, the qadi gets authority of disposal; considering the difference, the creditor is deprived of the right to take, acting on both similarities. Unlike commodities, because purpose relates to their forms and specific identities; currency is merely a means, so they differ. For the debt, currency is sold first, then commodities, then real estate. Begin with the easier, because it expedites repayment while considering the debtor's interest. And he is left with one set (dast) of the clothes of his body, and the rest is sold, because that suffices. And it is said: two sets, because when he washes his clothes, he must have clothing to wear.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling with proof on the qadi's authority to repay debts. It is divided into two parts: (1) When the debtor's debt and his property are of the same currency (both dirhams), the qadi may pay the creditor directly without the debtor's consent, by consensus, because the creditor has the right to take his due without permission, and the qadi acts as the state authority to enforce this. (2) When the currency differs (e.g., debt in dirhams but property in dinars), Abu Hanifah rules by istihsan that the qadi may sell the different currency to pay the debt, whereas qiyas would prohibit it as in the case of commodities (where forced sale is not allowed). The reasoning for istihsan: dirhams and dinars share the attribute of being prices (thumaniyyah) and monetary value (maliyyah), so the qadi has authority to dispose of them; but they differ in form, so the creditor cannot forcibly seize them (similar to commodities). Since the qadi acts with due consideration of both aspects, he may sell. The passage then discusses the order of sale: the qadi sells first currency (nuqud), then commodities (urud), then real estate (aqar), starting with the easiest for both repayment and the debtor's interest. The debtor is allowed to keep one set (dast) of clothing, and the rest is sold. There is also a view that two sets are allowed so he can wash one and wear the other.

Key Terms
ثمنية (thumaniyyah): The quality of being a price (medium of exchange/currency); applied to gold and silver coins.
مالية (maliyyah): Monetary value; the intrinsic worth that makes something wealth.
عروض (urud): Commodities/goods that are not gold or silver currency; items of specific identity.
دست (dast): A set of clothing; the minimum clothing allowed to be retained by the debtor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifah, why can the qadi sell dinars to pay a dirham debt but the creditor cannot seize them forcibly?
A) Because the qadi has more authority than the creditor
B) Because dirhams and dinars share monetary value but differ in form
C) Because the debtor agrees to the sale
D) Because dinars are like commodities

Key Takeaway
The qadi's authority to sell different currency by istihsan balances enforcement of debt with protection of the debtor's specific property.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

10 min
Qadi Selling Debtor's Different Currency Assets (Istihsan)
Ruling with proof: istihsan on selling different currencies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the qadi selling a debtor's currency of a different type, explain the istihsan reasoning, and distinguish it from the qiyas on goods.

Core Idea
Different currencies can be sold by the qadi because they share thamaniyya (monetary character) despite differing in form.

When a debtor owes dirhams but possesses dinars (or vice versa), the qadi has the authority to sell the debtor's currency to settle the debt. This ruling, according to Abu Hanifa, is based on istihsan — a departure from strict analogy. In this lesson, you will unpack the reasoning behind this juristic preference and see how it balances the rights of the creditor and debtor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage covers three cases: (a) debt and assets same currency (ijma', no sale needed), (b) different currencies (istihsan, sale allowed), (c) goods and real estate (qiyas forbids sale). Focus on (b).
Case (a) is settled by consensus; case (c) involves an intra-Hanafi dispute covered in another lesson.
2
Understand the qiyas argument
Strict analogy (qiyas) between currency and goods would forbid selling currency without consent. Goods cannot be sold because they are valued for their specific form and utility; currency would also be protected similarly.
This is the default rule (qiyas jali) that istihsan overrides.
3
Grasp the istihsan reason
Currency shares thamaniyya (monetary character) and maliyya (value) despite different form. Unity in those gives the qadi authority to sell; difference in form prevents the creditor from taking directly. This balance is the 'shabahayn' (two similarities) approach.
The hidden analogy (qiyas khafi) based on function is stronger than the apparent analogy based on form.
4
Apply the distinction from goods
Goods are valued for their specific form and essence (صورها واعيانها), so the qadi cannot sell them without consent. Currency is a mere means (وسائل), so the istihsan applies only to currency.
This distinction is key to understanding Abu Hanifa's position versus the Sahibayn.
5
Test the ruling with a modern parallel
If a debtor has US dollars but owes Euros, the qadi can sell the dollars to repay the Euros because both are currencies (thamaniyya). The reasoning remains valid despite modern changes in currency exchange.
Caution: actual court procedures may involve formal conversion, but the legal principle of istihsan applies.

صاحب ہدایہ نے و هذا عند ابی حنیفہ کہ کر ذکر امام ابوحنیفہ کی تصریح کی ہے۔ حالانکہ حکم ند لورا جماعی ہے۔ اس کی بابت بہت سے شراح ہدایہ نے یہ کہا ہے کہ چونکہ امام ابو حنیفہ قاضی کو مدیون کا اسباب فروخت کرنے کی اجازت نہیں دیتے تو شبہ امام ابو حنیفہ کے قول
If the debtor's debt is dirhams and he has dirhams, the qadi pays without his order; this is by consensus because the creditor has the right to take without consent, so the qadi may assist. If his debt is dirhams and he has dinars, or vice versa, the qadi sells them for his debt; this is istihsan according to Abu Hanifa. Qiyas would be that he does not sell, as with goods, and thus the creditor cannot take forcefully. The reason for istihsan: they are united in monetary character (thamaniyya) and value (maliyya) but different in form. Considering unity, the qadi has authority to transact; considering difference, the creditor lacks authority to take; acting on both similarities. Unlike goods, where purpose relates to their form and essence, whereas currency is merely a means, so they differ. In debt, currency is sold first, then goods, then real estate, beginning with the easiest because it hastens debt repayment while considering the debtor. One set of his body clothes is left, and the rest is sold because that suffices. It is said two sets, because when he washes his clothes, he needs something to wear. (Then follows Urdu commentary and explanation).
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage provides the rulings on how the qadi should handle a debtor's assets to repay debt. It covers three cases: (1) When the debt and assets are of the same currency (dirhams), the qadi can pay directly without the debtor's permission by ijma', because the creditor already has the right to take that exact property. (2) When the debt and assets are of different currencies (e.g., dirhams vs dinars), Abu Hanifa rules by istihsan that the qadi may sell the debtor's currency to repay the debt, even though qiyas would forbid it (like with goods). The reasoning is that all currencies share the quality of being mediums of exchange and value (thamaniyya and maliyya), so the qadi's authority to transact is established due to this unity, while the creditor's right to take without consent is negated due to difference in form. This balance is struck. (3) For goods and real estate, Abu Hanifa holds that the qadi cannot sell them without the debtor's permission, but the Sahibayn and the three other imams allow it. The order of sale is: currency first, then goods, then real estate, always beginning with the easiest to sell. Finally, one set of the debtor's essential clothing is left unsold, with a second set recommended if needed for washing.

Key Terms
ثمنية (thamaniyya): The quality of being a price or medium of exchange; monetary character.
مالية (maliyya): Property value; the quality of being valuable wealth.
استحسان (istihsan): A legal preference departing from strict qiyas due to a stronger consideration (e.g., unity in thamaniyya).
عروض (urud): Goods or commodities other than currency and real estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the qadi's authority to sell dinars to repay a dirham debt according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The two are the same form
B) They share thamaniyya and maliyya
C) The creditor has the right to take any asset
D) The qadi has unlimited authority

Key Takeaway
The qadi may sell a debtor's different-currency assets because currencies are united in monetary character and value, justifying istihsan over qiyas.

Order of Liquidation and Clothing Exemption for Debtor
Ruling with proof: order of liquidation and clothing exemption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the order in which a qadi sells a debtor's assets (currency, goods, real estate) and the rule on leaving essential clothing for the debtor.

Core Idea
The qadi sells the easiest assets first, and leaves one set of clothes for the debtor, with a second set recommended.

When a qadi must liquidate a debtor's assets to repay a debt, a specific order is followed: currency, then goods, then real estate, always starting with the most easily sold. Additionally, the debtor's basic clothing is protected from sale. This lesson covers these practical rules that balance swift debt repayment with the debtor's dignity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the order of sale
The qadi sells currency first, then goods, then real estate. This is to hasten repayment while being considerate to the debtor (selling items with less personal attachment first).
The principle is beginning with the easiest (aysar) to sell.
2
Understand the 'easiest' principle
The qadi chooses the most liquid or least harmful asset to sell. Currency is most liquid; goods less so; real estate is the most invasive. This minimizes hardship on the debtor.
This is based on maslaha and ihsan.
3
Apply the clothing exemption
One set of the debtor's body clothes (dast, a suit/outfit) is left unsold. Some say two sets because when one set is washed, the other is needed for wear.
This is to preserve basic human dignity and necessity (darurah).
4
Distinguish between enough and more
The primary view is one set suffices (kifayah). The second view (two sets) is based on the need for clean clothes. Both are within the Hanafi school.
The difference is a minor ikhtilaf within the school.
5
Connect to broader principles
These rules reflect the Hanafi concern for the debtor's welfare even when enforcing the creditor's right. It aligns with maxims like 'la darar wa la dirar' and 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha'.
The goal is justice without oppression.

صاحب ہدایہ نے و هذا عند ابی حنیفہ کہ کر ذکر امام ابوحنیفہ کی تصریح کی ہے۔ حالانکہ حکم ند لورا جماعی ہے۔ اس کی بابت بہت سے شراح ہدایہ نے یہ کہا ہے کہ چونکہ امام ابو حنیفہ قاضی کو مدیون کا اسباب فروخت کرنے کی اجازت نہیں دیتے تو شبہ امام ابو حنیفہ کے قول
If the debtor's debt is dirhams and he has dirhams, the qadi pays without his order; this is by consensus because the creditor has the right to take without consent, so the qadi may assist. If his debt is dirhams and he has dinars, or vice versa, the qadi sells them for his debt; this is istihsan according to Abu Hanifa. Qiyas would be that he does not sell, as with goods, and thus the creditor cannot take forcefully. The reason for istihsan: they are united in monetary character (thamaniyya) and value (maliyya) but different in form. Considering unity, the qadi has authority to transact; considering difference, the creditor lacks authority to take; acting on both similarities. Unlike goods, where purpose relates to their form and essence, whereas currency is merely a means, so they differ. In debt, currency is sold first, then goods, then real estate, beginning with the easiest because it hastens debt repayment while considering the debtor. One set of his body clothes is left, and the rest is sold because that suffices. It is said two sets, because when he washes his clothes, he needs something to wear. (Then follows Urdu commentary and explanation).
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage provides the rulings on how the qadi should handle a debtor's assets to repay debt. It covers three cases: (1) When the debt and assets are of the same currency (dirhams), the qadi can pay directly without the debtor's permission by ijma', because the creditor already has the right to take that exact property. (2) When the debt and assets are of different currencies (e.g., dirhams vs dinars), Abu Hanifa rules by istihsan that the qadi may sell the debtor's currency to repay the debt, even though qiyas would forbid it (like with goods). The reasoning is that all currencies share the quality of being mediums of exchange and value (thamaniyya and maliyya), so the qadi's authority to transact is established due to this unity, while the creditor's right to take without consent is negated due to difference in form. This balance is struck. (3) For goods and real estate, Abu Hanifa holds that the qadi cannot sell them without the debtor's permission, but the Sahibayn and the three other imams allow it. The order of sale is: currency first, then goods, then real estate, always beginning with the easiest to sell. Finally, one set of the debtor's essential clothing is left unsold, with a second set recommended if needed for washing.

Key Terms
دست (dast): A set or suit of clothes; a garment covering the body.
نقود (nuqud): Currency, money (coins).
عروض (urud): Goods or commodities other than currency and real estate.
عقار (aqar): Real estate, immovable property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many sets of a debtor's body clothes must be left according to the primary Hanafi view?
A) None
B) One set
C) Two sets
D) All clothes

Key Takeaway
The qadi sells assets in order of ease and must leave one set of the debtor's clothes to preserve basic human dignity.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Selling Debtor's Property: Cash vs. Goods and Order of Sale
Explains qadi's authority to sell debtor's property, ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn, and order of sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on selling a debtor's cash versus goods, and explain the role of istihsan in overriding qiyas.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa allows the qadi to sell debtor's cash by istihsan but not goods; Sahibayn allow both by qiyas – the difference turns on whether the asset is a means or an end.

This passage from al-Hidayah discusses the qadi's authority to sell a debtor's property to satisfy debts. The default rule (qiyas) would prohibit selling any property without the debtor's consent. However, a concession (istihsan) is made for cash (dirhams/dinars) because they are mere mediums of exchange. The Sahibayn and other imams extend this to goods as well, leading to a notable intra-Hanafi and cross-madhhab dispute. Let us dissect the reasoning and practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The core question: may the qadi sell the debtor's assets (cash and goods) to repay debts without the debtor's consent?
The dispute focuses on goods; cash is partially allowed by istihsan.
2
State the Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa: qadi can sell cash (by istihsan) but not goods. Sahibayn: qadi can sell both cash and goods (by qiyas).
The three other imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) agree with Sahibayn.
3
Analyze the proofs (dalil)
Abu Hanifa's dalil: goods are desired for their specific essence and form; the debtor's right attaches to the specific item. Cash is only a means (thaman), so selling it does not violate the debtor's interest. Sahibayn's dalil: the qadi has general authority to liquidate any non-essential property.
The reasoning involves a dual consideration: unity in being currency (authority for qadi) and difference in form (no forced taking by creditor).
4
Understand istihsan override
Abu Hanifa applies istihsan to override qiyas for cash: qiyas would forbid all sales, but istihsan permits cash sales because cash is merely a means and its sale does not harm the debtor's specific interests.
Istihsan is a distinctive Hanafi tool; it does not apply to goods because their specific form matters.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If the debtor has only goods, according to Abu Hanifa the qadi cannot force their sale; the creditor must wait or the debtor must sell them. According to Sahibayn, the qadi can sell the goods directly. Also, the order of sale: first cash, then goods, then property, starting with the most liquid.
The order ensures quick repayment while considering the debtor's hardship.
