Buying with Excessive Loss by an Authorized Slave: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ghubn fahish

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on ghubn fahish by an authorized slave and identify the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf) of the dispute.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa valid; Sahibayn invalid: excessive loss turns sale into donation.

This passage presents an internal Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a sale with excessive loss (ghubn fahish) conducted by an authorized slave. Abu Hanifa holds that any trade, even at a loss, is covered by authorization, while his two disciples consider it akin to a gift and thus invalid without specific permission. Understanding this disagreement sharpens your ability to analyze the scope of agency (wakalah) and the limits of trade.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute and its parties
State the exact issue: the validity of sale with excessive loss by an authorized slave. Name Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
2
Extract each position's rule and reasoning
For Abu Hanifa: the sale is valid because it is trade and the slave acts with his own capacity. For Sahibayn: it is like a donation, not covered by general authorization, citing the analogy to a sick person's donation.
3
Analyze the qiyas used by Sahibayn
Sahibayn analogize to a sick free person's donation limited to one-third. Abu Hanifa's response: a slave has no heirs, so the analogy fails; the slave's capacity is full.
This is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy).
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the buyer acquires valid title even with huge loss. Under Sahibayn, the master can void the sale if not specifically authorized.
5
Apply the rule to the terminal illness case
The passage extends the discussion: if the slave shows partiality (muhabah) during terminal illness, it is effective from all his wealth if no debt; otherwise it is from the remainder.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولو باع او اشترى بالغبن اليسير فهو جائز لتعذر الاحتراز عنه وكذا بالفاحش عند ابي حنيفة خلافا لهما هما يقولان ان البيع بالفاحش منه بمنزلة التبرع حتى اعتبر المريض من ثلث ماله فلا ينتظمه الاذن كالهبة وله انه تجارة والعبد متصرف باهلية نفسه فصار كالحر وعلى هذا الخلاف الصبى الماذون ولو حابي في مرض موته يعتبر من جميع ماله اذا لم يكن عليه دين وان كان فمن جميع ما بقى لان الاقتصار في الحر على الثلث المحق الورثة ولا وارث للعبد واذا كان الدين محيطا بما في يده يقال للمشترى جميع المحاباة والا فاردد البيع كما في الحر
He said: And if he sells or buys with minor loss (ghubn yasir), it is permissible because it is unavoidable. Similarly, with excessive loss (fahish) according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). They say that sale with excessive loss is like a donation (tabarru'); it is considered from one-third of a sick person's wealth, so authorization does not cover it, like a gift. His (Abu Hanifa's) proof is that it is trade, and the slave acts with his own capacity, so he becomes like a free person. The authorized minor is subject to the same difference. And if he acts partially (haba) in his terminal illness, it is considered from all his wealth if he has no debt, and if there is debt, from all that remains. Because restriction to one-third in the free person is to protect heirs, and the slave has no heirs. And if the debt encompasses all his holdings, the buyer is told: bring all the partiality (i.e., pay the full value) or return the sale, as in the case of a free person.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a sale involving excessive loss (ghubn fahish) performed by an authorized slave. There are three positions: Abu Hanifa holds such a sale is valid because the slave is capable and it is trade; the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it similar to a donation, which is not covered by general authorization. The text then discusses the case where the slave shows partiality (mahaba) in a sale during his terminal illness: if he has no debt, the transaction is effective but taken from all his wealth (because no heirs); if there is debt, it is taken from the remaining property after debt, or the buyer must either pay the full price or cancel. This reflects the principle that the slave's assets are for creditors, not heirs.

Key Terms
غبن فاحش (ghubn fahish): Excessive loss or overpayment in a sale, beyond normal market variation.
محاباة (muhabah): Showing partiality by selling below market value or buying above; a form of gift in disguise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the slave has full ownership of the goods.
B) Because trade inherently includes all sales, and the slave acts with his own capacity.
C) Because the master is liable for all debts.
D) Because the loss is insignificant.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's relied-upon position is that an authorized slave's sale with excessive loss is valid, as it remains a trade transaction.

Additional Permissible Acts for Authorized Slave
Listing various permissible dispositions for an authorized slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the additional permissible dispositions for an authorized slave according to the Hanafi school and explain why ijarah al-nafs is allowed despite the Shafi'i objection.

Core Idea
An authorized slave's person is his capital; hiring himself out is permissible unless it nullifies the authorization.

This passage expands the scope of an authorized slave's trading activities beyond simple buying and selling. It lists a range of permissible acts—salam, agency, pledging, leasing, sharecropping, partnerships, and even hiring himself out—all based on trade custom. The Hanafi school justifies these under the principle that the slave's trading capacity covers all customary trade accessories, except where the act would revoke the authorization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the listed acts
Read through the passage and note all the specific acts mentioned as permissible for the authorized slave.
There are at least nine distinct acts.
2
Categorize the acts
Group them into core trade acts (buy/sell, salam), trade accessories (rahn, wakala, partnership, mudarabah), and labor contracts (ijarah, muzara'ah, leasing land/houses).
This helps see the underlying principle.
3
Understand the general principle
The author ties each act to trade (tijarah) or its customary accompaniments. The permission springs from the slave's authorization to engage in trade.
Any act that falls under trade or is a necessary accessory is allowed.
4
Analyze the dispute on ijarah al-nafs
Focus on the specific ruling regarding hiring oneself out. Identify the Shafi'i objection and the Hanafi response.
The Hanafi proof: the slave's person is his capital; hiring does not nullify authorization.
5
Note the limits
The passage explicitly states exceptions: selling oneself or pledging oneself would nullify authorization, so those are not allowed.
This boundary preserves the master's purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله ان يسلم ويقبل السلم لانه تجارة وله ان يوكل بالبيع والشراء لانه قد لا يتفرغ بنفسه قال ويرهن ويرتهن لانهما من توابع التجارة فانهما ايفاء واستيفاء ويملك ان يتقبل الارض ویستاجر الاجراء والبيوت لان كل ذلك من صنيع التجارة ويأخذ الارض مزارعة لان فيه تحصيل الربح ويشترى طعاما فيزرعه فى ارضه لانه يقصد به الربح قال عليه السلام الزارع يتاجر ربه وله ان يشارك شركة عنان ويدفع المال مضاربة ويأخذها لانه من عادة التجار وله ان يواجر نفسه عندنا خلافا للشافعي وهو يقول لا يملك العقد على نفسه فكذا على منافعه لانها تابعة لها ولنا ان نفسه راس ماله فیملک التصرف فيها الا اذا كان يتضمن ابطال الاذن كالبيع لانه ينحجر به والرهن لانه يحبس به فلا يحصل مقصود المولى اما الاجارة لا ينحجر به ويحصل به المقصود وهو الربح فیملک
And he may give and accept salam (advance payment for future delivery) because it is trade. And he may appoint an agent for sale and purchase because he may not be personally free. He said: And he may give and take a pledge (rahn) because they are among the accessories of trade, as they are payment and receipt. And he has the right to accept land on lease (taqabbul al-ard), hire workers and houses, because all that is from the practice of trade. And he may take land for sharecropping (muzara'ah) because it involves obtaining profit. And he may buy grain and sow it in his land because he intends profit thereby. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The cultivator trades with his Lord.' And he may enter into a partnership of 'inan (shirkat 'inan) and give capital for mudarabah and take it, because that is from the custom of traders. And he may hire himself out (yu'ajjir nafsahu) according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. He (al-Shafi'i) says: He does not have authority to contract on his own person, so similarly on his usufructs, because they are subordinate to the person. And our proof is that his person is his capital; so he has the right to dispose of it, except when that entails nullifying the authorization, such as selling (himself) because he would be restrained, and pledging (himself) because he would be detained, and the master's purpose would not be achieved. As for hiring himself out, it does not restrain him, and the purpose (profit) is achieved, so he may do it.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage enumerates numerous permissible acts for the authorized slave (ma'dhun) that go beyond simple buying and selling. They include salam (advance sale), agency (wakala), pledging and taking pledges (rahn), leasing land, hiring laborers and houses, sharecropping (muzara'ah), growing crops, partnerships (shirkat 'inan), and mudarabah (silent partnership). All these are considered part of trade or its customary accessories. A notable point: hiring oneself out (ijarah al-nafs) is permissible according to the Hanafis, but Imam al-Shafi'i prohibits it because he considers the slave's usufructs tied to his person. The Hanafi reasoning: the slave's person is his capital; he can freely dispose of it except where it would negate the authorization (e.g., selling or pledging himself). Hiring does not restrain him from trading and actually brings profit, so it is allowed. A hadith about the cultivator trading with his Lord is cited.

Key Terms
سلم (salam): A sale with advance payment for future delivery of specified goods, considered a trade transaction.
إجارة النفس (ijarah al-nafs): Hiring oneself out to work for wages; the slave's person is his capital.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The authorized slave's trading capacity extends to all customary trade acts, including hiring himself out, as long as the act does not revoke his authorization.

Ruling on Ghabn Fahish and Muhabah in Illness for Authorized Slave
Dispute on ghabn fahish and ruling on muhabah in terminal illness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on ghabn fahish for an authorized slave and distinguish it from the Sahibayn's view, and apply the ruling on muhabah in terminal illness.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: ghabn fahish is trade; Sahibayn: it is a gift. The difference determines validity.

In this lesson, we explore a key intra-Hanafi dispute: Is a sale with excessive loss by an authorized slave valid, or is it akin to a gift? The answer determines whether the transaction is binding. We also examine the special case of muhabah (favoring the buyer) in terminal illness, where the slave's wealth is treated differently from a free person's. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly applying Hanafi fiqh in trade and agency contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The issue is whether an authorized slave's sale with excessive loss (ghabn fahish) is valid or considered a gift. Also, how is muhabah treated in terminal illness?
Ghabn fahish is a loss significantly exceeding normal market variation.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds the sale is valid because it is trade, and the slave acts by his own capacity, like a free person. The slave is authorized for trade, and ghabn fahish does not change the nature of the transaction.
His proof: the slave is a trader, not a donor.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it a gift (tabarru'), not covered by trade permission. They analogize to a sick person's gift limited to one-third of wealth.
Their proof: excessive loss indicates donative intent.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa responds that it remains trade because it occurs in the trade context; the slave's capacity makes him like a free person, for whom such a sale is valid despite loss.
The difference is in characterizing the transaction.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If the sale is invalid per Sahibayn, the slave can rescind. According to Abu Hanifa, the sale is binding. Also, in terminal illness, muhabah is from the entire wealth (no heirs), not limited to one-third.
Practical consequence: validity and enforceability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمہ اگر اس نے بچایا خرید ا خفیف خسارہ کے ساتھ تو یہ جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ اس سے احتر از معد ر ہے ایسے ہی فاحش خسارہ کے ساتھ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک بر خلاف صاحبین کے۔ وہ یہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اس کی طرف سے فاحش خسارہ کے ساتھ بیچنا بمنزلہ تبرع کے ہے یہاں تک کہ مریض کی طرف سے یہ اس کے تہائی مال سے معتبر ہوتا ہے تو اجازت اس کو شامل نہ ہوگی ہبہ کی طرح امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ بھی تجارت ہے اور غلام اپنی ذاتی لیاقت سے متصرف ہے تو آزاد کی طرح ہو گیا۔ طفل ماذون بھی اسی اختلاف پر ہے اگر اس نے اپنے مرض الموت میں محابات کی تو یہ اس کے تمام مال سے معتبر ہوگی۔ اگر اس کے ذمہ قرضہ نہ ہو تو پورے باقی ماندہ سے معتبر ہوگی۔ کیونکہ آزاد کے حق میں تہائی پر اقتصار حق ورثہ کی وجہ سے ہے اور غلام کا کوئی وارث نہیں ہے اور جب قرضہ اس کے تمام مقبوضہ مال کو محیط ہو تو مشتری سے کہا جائے گا کہ پوری محابات ادا کرو نہ بیچ پھیر دے جیسے آزاد کی صورت میں ہوتا ہے
Translation: If he sells and buys with a slight loss, it is permissible, because avoiding it is difficult. Similarly, with excessive loss, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, unlike the two companions (Sahibayn). They say that selling with excessive loss by him is like a gift (tabarru'), to the extent that if done by a sick person, it is considered from one-third of his wealth, so permission does not include it, like a gift. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that this is also trade, and the slave acts based on his own capacity, so he becomes like a free person. The authorized child (tifl ma'dhun) is also subject to this difference. If he makes muhabah in his terminal illness, it is considered from all his wealth if he has no debt; otherwise from the remainder. Because for a free person, restriction to one-third is for the right of heirs, and the slave has no heirs. If debt encompasses all his possessed wealth, the buyer is told to pay the full muhabah or return the sale, as in the case of a free person.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the Hidayah discussing the authorized slave's sale with profit or loss. It first states that sale with slight loss is permissible due to necessity. Then it presents a dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding excessive loss (ghabn fahish). The Sahibayn argue that such a sale is akin to a gift (tabarru'), which is not included in the permission. They analogize to a sick person whose gift is limited to one-third of his wealth. Abu Hanifa counters that it remains a trade, and the slave's capacity makes him analogous to a free person, for whom such a sale is valid. The commentary then extends the dispute to an authorized child (tifl ma'dhun). It further rules on muhabah (favoring the buyer) in terminal illness: it is valid from the entire wealth if no debt, otherwise from the remainder, since there are no heirs to protect. If debt covers all assets, the buyer must pay the full muhabah or rescind the sale.

Key Terms
غبن فاحش (ghabn fahish): Excessive loss in a sale, where the price is significantly below market value.
محابات (muhabah): Favoring the buyer by selling below market value, often in terminal illness.
تبرع (tabarru'): A voluntary gift or donation, not a trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is valid only if the slave is not in debt.

Key Takeaway
An authorized slave's sale with excessive loss is valid trade according to Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn treat it as a gift; the slave's terminal illness muhabah is from entire wealth due to absence of heirs.

Permissible Trade Transactions for the Authorized Slave
List of valid transactions and justification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the transactions permitted for an authorized slave and explain the justification that they are adjuncts of trade.

Core Idea
The authorized slave may engage in any trade-related transaction that does not nullify his permission.

This passage from the Hidayah enumerates specific transactions that an authorized slave (al-ma'dhun) may undertake. These include pledging, leasing, sharecropping, partnership, and mudarabah. All are justified as necessary for trade. The key principle is that the slave acts as a trader; his authority extends to what serves trade.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transactions listed
Read the passage and list all actions mentioned: rahn (pledging), ijarah (leasing), muzara'ah (sharecropping), shirkat 'inan (partnership), mudarabah (silent partnership).
Also includes buying seeds and cultivating.
2
Classify the justification
Each transaction is justified as a 'tabi' al-tijarah' (adjunct of trade) or because it involves profit.
The hadith 'The cultivator trades with his Lord' supports cultivation as trade.
3
Note the exception
The text explicitly excludes actions that nullify the permission, such as selling oneself or pledging oneself.
These actions restrict the slave and defeat the master's objective.
4
Apply the principle
Understand that the permission is for trade; any trade-related act that does not hinder the master's objective is allowed.
The list is illustrative, not exhaustive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ويرهن ويرتهن لانهما من توابع التجارة فانهما ايفاء واستيفاء ويملك ان يتقبل الارض ويستاجر الاجراء والبيوت لان كل ذلك من صنيع التجارة ويأخذ الارض مزارعة لان فيه تحصيل الربح ويشترى طعاما فيزرعه فى ارضه لانه يقصد به الربح قال عليه السلام الزارع يتاجر ربه وله ان يشارك شركة عنان ويدفع المال مضاربة ويأخذها لانه من عادة التجار وله ان يواجر نفسه عندنا خلافا للشافعي وهو يقول لا يملك العقد على نفسه فكذا على منافعه لانها تابعة لها ولنا ان نفسه راس ماله فيملك التصرف فيها الا اذا كان يتضمن ابطال الاذن كالبيع لانه ينحجر به والرهن لانه يحبس به فلا يحصل مقصود المولى اما الاجارة لا ينحجر به ويحصل به المقصود وهو الربح فيملك
He said: He may give and take in pledge (rahn), because both are adjuncts of trade—they are discharge and recovery. He has the right to lease land, hire workers and houses, because all that is part of trade operations. He may take land on sharecropping (muzara'ah) because it involves profit. He may buy food and cultivate it in his land because he intends profit thereby. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The cultivator trades with his Lord.' He may enter into a partnership of 'inan and give or take capital in mudarabah, because that is the custom of traders. He may lease himself (ijarah) according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i, who says he does not have the authority to contract on himself, so neither on his usufructs because they follow the self. Our proof: his self is his capital, so he has the authority to dispose of it except what entails nullifying permission, such as selling himself (because it would restrict him) and pledging himself (because it would detain him), defeating the master's objective. As for ijarah, it does not restrict him and achieves the objective—profit—so he has authority.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This is the matn (core text) from the Hidayah listing various transactions permitted for the authorized slave. It begins by permitting giving and taking pledges, as they are necessary for trade. Then it allows leasing land, hiring workers and houses, sharecropping, buying seeds and cultivating, forming partnerships (shirkat 'inan), and mudarabah. A hadith is cited to support cultivation. The key point is the dispute over ijarah: the Hanafi school (at us: 'indana) permits the slave to lease himself (hire out his services), while Imam al-Shafi'i denies it because the slave cannot contract on his own person. The Hanafi argument is that the slave's person is his capital (ra's al-mal); he has disposal rights except where the permission would be voided (selling himself, pledging himself). Ijarah, however, does not restrict him and brings profit, so it is allowed.

Key Terms
إجارة (ijarah): Leasing oneself or hiring out one's services.
شركة عنان (shirkat 'inan): A partnership where each partner contributes capital and work, with equal or proportional sharing of profits.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Leasing land
B) Selling himself
C) Forming a partnership
D) Sharecropping

Key Takeaway
The authorized slave's permission extends to all trade-related acts that do not defeat the master's purpose.

The Ijarah Dispute: Can an Authorized Slave Lease Himself?
Inter-madhhab dispute on ijarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on ijarah for an authorized slave and the Shafi'i counter-argument, and evaluate the proof.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school permits an authorized slave to lease himself because his person is his capital; ijarah brings profit without restricting his trade.

This passage presents a famous inter-madhhab dispute. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that an authorized slave cannot lease himself because he lacks contractual capacity over his person. The Hanafi school disagrees, arguing that the slave's person is his capital (ra's al-mal) and ijarah is a profitable transaction that does not nullify the slave's permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The dispute is whether an authorized slave may lease himself (ijarah) – i.e., hire out his services.
The text says 'wa lahu an yojiri nafsahu 'indana khilafan lishafi'y'.
2
State the Shafi'i position and proof
Al-Shafi'i says the slave cannot contract on himself, so he cannot contract on his usufructs either. This is based on the principle that the usufruct follows the person.
The text summarizes: 'he does not have the authority to contract on himself, so neither on his usufructs because they follow the self.'
3
State the Hanafi position and proof
The Hanafi view: the slave's person is his capital (ra's al-mal). He has authority to dispose of it except where it nullifies the permission (like selling or pledging). Ijarah does not nullify permission and achieves profit.
The text says: 'his self is his capital, so he has the authority to dispose of it except what entails nullifying permission... as for ijarah, it does not restrict him and achieves the objective.'
4
Determine the practical consequence
If the slave leases himself, the contract is valid in the Hanafi school but void according to al-Shafi'i.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical fruit of the disagreement).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ويرهن ويرتهن لانهما من توابع التجارة فانهما ايفاء واستيفاء ويملك ان يتقبل الارض ويستاجر الاجراء والبيوت لان كل ذلك من صنيع التجارة ويأخذ الارض مزارعة لان فيه تحصيل الربح ويشترى طعاما فيزرعه فى ارضه لانه يقصد به الربح قال عليه السلام الزارع يتاجر ربه وله ان يشارك شركة عنان ويدفع المال مضاربة ويأخذها لانه من عادة التجار وله ان يواجر نفسه عندنا خلافا للشافعي وهو يقول لا يملك العقد على نفسه فكذا على منافعه لانها تابعة لها ولنا ان نفسه راس ماله فيملك التصرف فيها الا اذا كان يتضمن ابطال الاذن كالبيع لانه ينحجر به والرهن لانه يحبس به فلا يحصل مقصود المولى اما الاجارة لا ينحجر به ويحصل به المقصود وهو الربح فيملك
He said: He may give and take in pledge (rahn), because both are adjuncts of trade—they are discharge and recovery. He has the right to lease land, hire workers and houses, because all that is part of trade operations. He may take land on sharecropping (muzara'ah) because it involves profit. He may buy food and cultivate it in his land because he intends profit thereby. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The cultivator trades with his Lord.' He may enter into a partnership of 'inan and give or take capital in mudarabah, because that is the custom of traders. He may lease himself (ijarah) according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i, who says he does not have the authority to contract on himself, so neither on his usufructs because they follow the self. Our proof: his self is his capital, so he has the authority to dispose of it except what entails nullifying permission, such as selling himself (because it would restrict him) and pledging himself (because it would detain him), defeating the master's objective. As for ijarah, it does not restrict him and achieves the objective—profit—so he has authority.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This is the matn (core text) from the Hidayah listing various transactions permitted for the authorized slave. It begins by permitting giving and taking pledges, as they are necessary for trade. Then it allows leasing land, hiring workers and houses, sharecropping, buying seeds and cultivating, forming partnerships (shirkat 'inan), and mudarabah. A hadith is cited to support cultivation. The key point is the dispute over ijarah: the Hanafi school (at us: 'indana) permits the slave to lease himself (hire out his services), while Imam al-Shafi'i denies it because the slave cannot contract on his own person. The Hanafi argument is that the slave's person is his capital (ra's al-mal); he has disposal rights except where the permission would be voided (selling himself, pledging himself). Ijarah, however, does not restrict him and brings profit, so it is allowed.

Key Terms
إجارة (ijarah): Leasing oneself or hiring out one's services.
شركة عنان (shirkat 'inan): A partnership where each partner contributes capital and work, with equal or proportional sharing of profits.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi proof for permitting the authorized slave to lease himself?
A) The slave owns himself absolutely.
B) The master's permission implies it.
C) His person is his capital.
D) The Prophet explicitly allowed it.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the authorized slave may lease himself because his person is considered capital and ijarah achieves the master's objective of profit without restricting the slave.

Permission for a Slave: Waiver or Agency?
Dispute on whether permission for one type authorizes all types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi position (fakk al-hajr) from the opposing view (tawkil) and state the practical consequence for the authorized slave's transactions.

Core Idea
Permission for a slave is a waiver of restriction, not agency — so the slave is authorized in all trade types.

When a master permits a slave to engage in one type of trade (e.g., buying), does that limit the slave to that type, or does it open the door to all trade? The Hanafi school argues that permission is a removal of the master's right to restrict (fakk al-hajr), unleashing the slave's natural capacity. The opposing school views permission as agency (tawkil), restricting the slave to the scope given. This lesson dissects the legal basis and its real-world effect.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Clearly state that the Hanafi school holds the slave authorized in all trade types when permission is given for one type.
This is based on fakk al-hajr (waiver of restriction).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue the slave is limited to the specified type only.
They analogize permission to agency (tawkil).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand their reasoning: permission is like agency where authority derives from the master and ownership goes to the master, so the slave is restricted to what the master specifies.
They compare the slave to a mudarib who is limited to the partnership terms.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal: permission is a waiver (isqat), not a delegation. The slave's own ownership is manifest, so he is not limited.
The slave's transaction effects ownership for himself, not the master, distinguishing him from an agent.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: if the slave buys a house when only permitted to buy food, the Hanafis uphold the sale; the opponents require ratification.
This shows the real impact of the legal theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان اذن له فى نوع منها دون غيره فهو ماذون فى جميعها وقال زفر والشافعي لا يكون ماذونا الا في ذلك النوع وعلى هذا الخلاف اذا نها عن التصرف في نوع آخر لهما ان الاذن توكيل وانابة من المولى لانه يستفيد الولاية من جهته ويثبت الحكم وهو الملك له دون العبد ولهذا يملك حجره فيتخصص بما خصه كالمضارب ولنا انه اسقاط الحق وفك الحجر على ما بيناه وعند ذلك يظهر مالكية العبد فلا. يتخصص بنوع دون نوع بخلاف الوكيل لانه يتصرف في مال غيره فتثبت له الولاية من جهته وحكم التصرف وهو الملك واقع للعبد حتى كان له ان يصرفه الى قضاء الدين والنفقة وما استغنى عنه يخلفه
If he (master) permits him (slave) for one type among them (transactions) excluding others, then he is authorized in all of them. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: He is authorized only in that type. On this difference is the case when he prohibits him from transacting in another type. Their proof: permission is agency (tawkil) and deputation from the master, because he (slave) derives authority from him, and the legal effect (ownership) is established for the master, not the slave. Therefore, the master can restrict him, so he is limited to what he specified, like a mudarib. Our proof: permission is waiver of right and removal of restriction (fakk al-hajr), as we explained. Thereupon the slave's ownership becomes manifest, so he is not limited to one type rather than another, unlike the agent (wakil), because he transacts in another's property, so authority is derived from the master, and the effect (ownership) is for the slave, so he can apply it to debt payment, maintenance, and when he gains surplus, he keeps it.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute on whether an authorized slave is restricted to the specific type of transaction for which the master gave permission, or whether he is authorized in all types. The Hanafi position (Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad) holds that if the master permits one type (e.g., buying only), the slave is authorized in all trade types (selling, hiring, etc.). The opposing view of Zufar and al-Shafi'i is that he is limited to that type. The same difference applies if the master prohibits a specific type: Hanafis say the prohibition does not restrict the slave's authority in other types, while the opponents say it does. The opponents argue that permission is like agency (tawkil), where the agent's authority is limited to what is specified. The Hanafi response distinguishes: permission for an authorized slave is not agency but a waiver of the master's right to restrict (fakk al-hajr), allowing the slave to act as if he owns the property. Thus, the slave's ownership is recognized, and he can engage in any trade activity, as the effect of transactions (ownership) goes to him. An agent (wakil), by contrast, acts for another, so his authority is limited.

Key Terms
فك الحجر (fakk al-hajr): Removal of the legal restriction (hajr) that prevents the slave from transacting.
توكيل (tawkil): Agency; the principal appoints an agent to act on his behalf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a master permits his slave only to buy food, the slave may also:
A) Only buy food and nothing else.
B) Not sell any item.
C) Engage in any trade transaction, including selling.
D) Only buy food but may also hire workers for food purchase.

Key Takeaway
Permission for a slave (idhn) is fakk al-hajr (removal of restriction), not agency (tawkil), so the slave is authorized in all trade types, with significant practical consequences.

The Authorized Slave's Sale with Major Loss: Trade or Donation?
Inter-madhhab dispute on authorized slave's sale with major loss

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa validates an authorized slave's sale with major loss and evaluate the opposing argument that it resembles donation.

Core Idea
A sale with major loss by an authorized slave is valid as trade, not donation, because the loss is incidental to the contract.

This lesson examines a dispute over whether an authorized slave may sell goods at a major loss (ghubn fahish). While minor loss is unavoidable and agreed upon, Abu Hanifa holds that even a major loss does not invalidate the sale, as it remains a trade transaction. The opposing school (Sahibayn, Shafi'i, Ahmad) considers it akin to a donation, which exceeds the slave's authorization. We will unpack the qiyas-based arguments and their practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
What is the exact scenario? An authorized slave sells goods at a price significantly below market value. Is the sale valid?
The sale is a normal trade contract except for the loss.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
Abu Hanifa says valid. Proof: The sale is a trade contract; the loss is incidental. The slave transacts based on his capability, like a free man.
The key is that the contract's nature (trade) governs, not the outcome.
3
State the opposing position and its proof
Sahibayn, Zufar, Shafi'i, Ahmad say invalid. Proof: Major loss resembles donation (tabarru'), which is not authorized. Analogy: a sick person's donation is limited to one-third.
They see the loss as the dominant element, transforming the transaction.
4
Analyze the qiyas (analogical reasoning)
Both sides use qiyas but with different asl and illa. Identify the structure: Hanafi asl = free man's trade; opponent's asl = donation. Which illa is more appropriate?
Focus on the effective cause: is it the contract type or the degree of loss?
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the sale is valid and binding on the master. Under opponents, it is void (batil) and must be annulled.
Practical consequence: the buyer may keep the goods or must return them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشریح قوله بالغبن اليسر..... الخ - اگر عبد مازون نے خفیف سے خسارہ کے ساتھ خرید وفروخت کی تو یہ جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ اس سے احتراز متعذر ہے ( امام شافعی اور احمد کے نزدیک صرف شمن مثل ہی کے ساتھ جائز ہے) بلکہ اگر غبن فاحش کے ساتھ ہوتو امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک تب بھی جائز ہے۔ صاحبین ، امام زفر، امام شافعی، احمد کے نزدیک جائز نہیں۔ وہ یہ فرماتے ہیں کہ عبد ماذون کی طرف سے فاحش خسارہ کے ساتھ بیع کرنا بمنزلہ تبرع کے ہے جو خلاف مقصود ہے۔ کیونکہ مقصود تو استر باح ہے نہ کہ اتلاف حتی کہ اگر مریض ایسا کرے تو اس کے تہائی مال سے معتبر ہوتا ہے۔ پس آقا کی اجازت اس کو شامل نہ ہوگی ۔ جیسے ہبہ کو شامل نہیں ہے۔ قوله وله انه تجارة ... الخ - امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ بیع غبن فاحش بھی تجارت ہے نہ کہ تبرع اس لئے کہ اس کا وقوع عقد تجارت کے ضمن میں ہوا ہے اور جو چیز کسی شی کے ضمن میں واقع ہو اس کا حکم اسی شی کا ہوتا ہے۔ پھر عبد ماذون اپنی لیاقت سے متصرف ہوتا ہے تو اس کا حکم آزاد کے مثل ہو گیا۔ کہ آزاد آدمی کی غبن فاحش کے ساتھ بیع جائز ہے تو عبد مازون کی بیع بھی جائز ہوگی۔
Explanation of his statement 'with minor loss...' - If an authorized slave buys or sells with a slight loss, it is permissible, because avoiding it is impossible. (According to Imam Shafi'i and Ahmad, it is only permissible at the market price.) Rather, if the loss is major, according to Imam Abu Hanifa it is still permissible. According to Sahibayn, Imam Zufar, Imam Shafi'i, and Ahmad, it is not permissible. They say that an authorized slave's sale with major loss is akin to donation, which contradicts the purpose. Because the purpose is profit, not wastage; even if a sick person does it, it is considered from one-third of his wealth. Thus, the master's permission does not include it, just as donation is not included. His statement 'and he has that it is trade...' - Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that a sale with major loss is still trade, not donation, because it occurs within a trade contract, and whatever occurs within a thing takes the ruling of that thing. Then the authorized slave transacts based on his capability, so his ruling becomes like that of a free man. A free man's sale with major loss is permissible, so the authorized slave's sale is also permissible.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute. The commentator explains the ruling on an authorized slave's sale involving a loss (ghubn). He first states that a slight loss is unavoidable and thus permissible by consensus, though Shafi'i and Ahmad restrict to market price. Then the dispute arises over major loss: Abu Hanifa deems it valid because it is still a trade transaction, not a donation, and the slave controls his own capability like a free man. The opposing view (Sahibayn, Zufar, Shafi'i, Ahmad) considers it a donation (tabarru'), which is not within the scope of authorization, drawing analogy to a terminally ill person whose donations are limited to one-third. The text then presents Abu Hanifa's specific proof: the major loss occurs within a trade contract, so it takes the ruling of trade; the slave's legal capacity is analogous to a free man. This is a clear example of Hanafi qiyas (analogical reasoning) prioritizing the nature of the contract over potential abuse.

Key Terms
غبن (ghubn): Loss or deficit in a transaction, i.e., selling for less than market value.
تبرع (tabarru'): A gratuitous transfer without consideration, like donation.

A) Because it is a donation

C) Because the slave is like a free man in all respects
D) Because the master explicitly permitted it

Key Takeaway
An authorized slave's sale with major loss is valid in the Hanafi school because the loss is incidental to the trade contract, not a separate donation.

Muhabat by Authorized Slave in Terminal Illness
Expansion of muhabat conditions for authorized slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid muhabat by an authorized slave in terminal illness and apply them to determine validity.

Core Idea
An authorized slave's muhabat is valid from his entire wealth because he has no heirs, unlike a free man limited to one-third.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on a favorable sale (muhabat) made by an authorized slave during his terminal illness. The slave, though authorized to trade, is restricted by his master's interest and his own debt. The key distinction from a free man's muhabat is the absence of heirs, which allows the slave to gift from all his wealth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is muhabat (favorable sale) by an authorized slave during his terminal illness. The ruling is that it is valid if certain conditions are met.
Muhabat combines sale and donation.
2
List the conditions for validity
Three conditions: (1) The muhabat amount must be within the slave's total wealth. (2) The master must be healthy. (3) The slave must have no debt.
If any condition is missing, the ruling changes.
3
Analyze the effect of debt
If the slave has debt, muhabat is only valid from the surplus after debt. If debt covers all wealth, muhabat becomes void, and the buyer must pay full value or return the sale.
Debt takes priority over gifts.
4
Compare to a free man's muhabat
A free man's muhabat in terminal illness is limited to one-third of his wealth due to heirs' rights. Since the slave has no heirs, his entire wealth is available.
This comparison clarifies the rationale.
5
Determine the consequence when void
If muhabat is void, the buyer has two options: pay the full price (without discount) or return the item and cancel the sale.
Same as free man's case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ولو حابى .. الخ - اگر عبد مازون نے اپنے مرض الموت میں محابات کی یعنی زیادہ قیمت کی چیز کم قیمت پر فروخت کی یا اس کی وصیت کی تاکہ مشتری کو کچھ عطیہ پہنچ جائے ۔ تو اگر اس کے کل مال سے مقدار محابات نکل سکتی ہو تو بیع جائز ہوگی۔ مثلاً ہزار کی چیز سات سو میں بچی تو یہ تین سو کی محابات ہے اور کل مال اسی قدر یا اس سے زیادہ ہے تو محابات جائز ہو گی ۔ بشرطیکہ آقا تندرست ہو اور غلام پر قرضہ نہ ہو ۔ اور اگر اس پر قرضہ ہو تو قرضہ کے بعد جو باقی رہے اس سے معتبر ہوگی اس لئے کہ آزاد کی صورت میں جو محابات صرف تہائی سے معتبر ہوتی ہے وہ حق ورثہ کی وجہ سے ہے اور غلام کا کوئی وارث نہیں ہے تو کل مال سے معتبر ہوگی اور اگر غلام پر دین محیط ہو تو محابات باطل ہو جائے گی اور مشتری سے کہا جائے گا کہ یا تو محابات کی پوری مقدار ادا کرور نہ بیچ کو رد کر جیسے اگر آزاد آدمی اپنے مرض الموت میں محابات کرے تو اس میں یہی ہوتا ہے۔
His statement 'and if he does muhabat...' - If an authorized slave in his terminal illness does muhabat, i.e., sells a valuable item at a low price or makes a bequest to give a gift to the buyer, then if the amount of muhabat can be taken from his entire wealth, the sale is valid. For example, if he sells a thousand-dirham item for seven hundred, this is a three-hundred dirham muhabat, and if his total wealth is that much or more, the muhabat is valid, provided that the master is healthy and the slave has no debt. If he has debt, it will be considered from what remains after the debt, because in the case of a free man, muhabat is only considered from one-third due to heirs' rights, but the slave has no heirs, so it is considered from all wealth. If the debt encompasses the wealth, the muhabat becomes void, and the buyer will be told to either pay the full amount of muhabat or return the sale, just as in the case of a free man's muhabat in terminal illness.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement explaining the conditions for validity of a 'muhabat' (favorable sale) by an authorized slave during his terminal illness (marad al-mawt). The commentator outlines several conditions: The muhabat amount must be within the slave's total wealth; the master must be healthy; and the slave must have no debt. If there is debt, the muhabat is only considered from the surplus after debt. If debt covers all wealth, the muhabat becomes void, and the buyer must either pay the full value or void the sale. The reason given is that a free man's muhabat is limited to one-third due to heirs, but the slave has no heirs, so his entire wealth is subject. This ruling shows the Hanafi approach to balancing the slave's right to trade with the master's interest and creditors' rights.

Key Terms
محاباة (muhabat): A sale where the seller gives a favorable price, essentially a gift element.
مرض الموت (marad al-mawt): Terminal illness that likely leads to death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The muhabat is void
B) The muhabat is valid
C) Only one-third is valid
D) The sale must be returned

Key Takeaway
An authorized slave's muhabat in terminal illness is valid from his entire wealth if he has no debt and the master is healthy; otherwise it is limited to surplus or void.

Scope of Permission for a Ma'dhun Slave: Hanafi vs. Zufar/Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on scope of ma'dhun permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi position on the scope of a ma'dhun's trade permission from that of Imam Zufar and al-Shafi'i, and explain the legal reasoning (talil) behind each.

Core Idea
Permission lifts restriction, revealing full ownership – therefore a ma'dhun cannot be limited to one trade type.

In Hanafi fiqh, the ma'dhun slave is not a mere agent of his master; he owns the profit and can transact freely. This lesson examines a key dispute: if a master permits only a specific type of trade, does the slave become restricted to that type? The Hanafi school says no, while Imam Zufar and al-Shafi'i disagree. Understanding this ikhtilaf clarifies the fundamental nature of idhn (permission) in slave trade.

Guided Path
1
Understand the preliminary ruling
Identify that if the master orders a specific item (e.g., a garment for clothing), the slave is not ma'dhun because that is personal service (istikhdam).
This contrasts with permission for a type of trade, which triggers the dispute.
2
State the core dispute
When the master permits a specific trade type (e.g., dyeing), does the slave become ma'dhun in all types or only that type? Hanafi says all; Zufar and Shafi'i say specific only.
3
Analyze the opposing school's qiyas
Examine how the opponents analogize idhn to wikala (agency) or mudaraba: the master retains ownership, so he can restrict the slave's authority.
The illa (effective cause) is that authority derives from the master and ownership goes to the master.
4
Examine the Hanafi refutation
Understand the Hanafi definition: idhn is fakk al-hajr (lifting restriction) and isqat al-haqq (dropping the master's right), revealing the slave's own ownership. Therefore, no restriction can be imposed.
The wakeel acts in another's property; the ma'dhun acts for himself and owns the profit.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah: in Hanafi view, the slave can trade in all types even if permitted only one; in the opposing view, he cannot. The Hanafi view is mufta bihi.
This also applies when the master prohibits a specific type.

Source Text

كتَابُ المَاذُونَ
المالك فيه قال وان اذن له فى شيء بعينه فليس بماذون لانه استخدام ومعناه ان يأمره بشراء ثوب للكسوة او طعام رزقاً لاهله وهذا لانه لو صار ماذونا ينسد عليه باب الاستخدام بخلاف ما اذا قال اد الى الغلة كل شهر كذا او قال اد الى الفا وانت حر لانه طلب منه المال ولا يحصل الا بالكسب او قال له اقعد صباغا او قصارا لانه اذن يشراء ما لابد منه لهما وهو نوع فيصير ماذونا فى الانواع قال واقرار الماذون بالديون والغصوب جائز وكذا بالودائع لان الاقرار من توابع التجارة اذ لو لم يصح لاجتنب الناس مبايعته ومعاملته ولا فرق بين ما اذا كان عليه دين او لم يكن اذا كان الاقرار فى صحته وان كان في مرضه يقدم دين الصحة كما فى الحر بخلاف الاقرار بما يجب من المال لا بسبب التجارة لانه كالمحجور في حقه. ترجمہ ... اگر اجازت دی اس کو کسی خاص نوع تجارت کی نہ کہ اس کے علاوہ کی تو وہ جمیع اقسام میں ماذون ہوگا۔ امام زفر و امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ ماذون نہ ہو گا مگر اسی نوع میں اور اسی اختلاف پر ہے جب اس کو دوسری قسم میں تصرف کرنے سے منع کر دیا ہو ان کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اذن آقا کی طرف سے تو کیل و انابت ہے کیونکہ وہ ولایت اس کی جانب سے حاصل کرتا ہے اور حکم یعنی ملک اسی کے لئے ثابت ہوتی ہے نہ کہ غلام کے لئے اس لئے آقا اس کو مجبور کرنے کا مختار ہوتا ہے پس اجازت اس کے ساتھ مختص ہوگی جس کی اس نے تخصیص کی ہے۔ مضارب کی طرح۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ اذن حق کا اسقاط اور حجر دور کرنا ہے اور اس وقت غلام کی مالکیت ظاہر ہو جاتی ہے پس کسی نوع کے ساتھ اختصاص نہ ہوگا بخلاف وکیل کے کیونکہ وہ مال غیر میں تصرف کرتا ہے تو اس کے لئے ولایت غیر کی جانب سے ثابت ہے اور تصرف کا حکم یعنی ملک خود غلام کے لئے واقع ہوتی ہے یہاں تک کہ اس کو اپنے اداء قرض اور نفقہ میں صرف کرنے کا اختیار ہوتا ہے اور جو بیچ رہے اس میں آقا اس کا خلیفہ ہوتا ہے اور اگر کوئی معین چیز خریدنے کی اجازت دی تو وہ ماذون نہ ہوگا کیونکہ یہ تو خدمت لینا ہوا اور اس کے معنی یہ ہیں کہ اس کو لباس کے لئے کپڑا خرید نے یا اپنے اہل وعیال کے لئے اناج خرید نے کا حکم دیا اور اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ اگر وہ اس سے بھی مازون ہو جائے تو خدمت لینے کا دروازہ بند ہو جائے گا بخلاف اس کے جب کہا کہ مجھے ماہانہ اتنا دیا کریا کہا کہ مجھے ہزار دیدے اور تو آزاد ہے کیونکہ آقا نے اس سے مال طلب کیا ہے اور یہ کمائی کے بغیر حاصل نہیں ہوسکتا یا غلام سے کہا کہ انگر یز یا دھوبی بھلا کیونکہ یہ اس چیز کے خریدنے کی اجازت ہے جو ان دونوں کے لئے ضروری ہے اور یہ ایک قسم ہے تو وہ جمیع انواع میں ماذون ہو جائے گا
بنانا ہوتا ہے کیونکہ عبد ماذون کو ولایت تصرف آقاہی کی جانب سے حاصل ہوتی ہے اور حکم یعنی ملکیت آقا کو حاصل ہوتی ہے نہ کہ عبد مازون کر۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ آقا کو اس کے مجبور کرنے کا اختیار ہوتا ہے لہذا جس نوع تجارت کے ساتھ آقا نے تخصیص کی ہے وہ تخصیص صحیح ہوگی جیسے مضارب کے حق میں صحیح ہوتی ہے۔
قوله ولنا انه اسقاط .... الخ - ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ اذن فک حجر اور اسقاط حق ہے جس کی تحقیق شروع میں گذر چکی اور جب آقا نے اپنا حق ساقط کر دیا اور حجر دور ہو گیا تو غلام کی مالکیت ظاہر ہو گئی تو کسی خاص نوع تجارت کے ساتھ اختصاص نہ ہوگا بخلاف وکیل کے کہ وہ دوسرے کے مال میں تصرف کیا کرتا ہے اور اس کو ولایت تصرف دوسرے کی طرف سے حاصل ہوتی ہے اور ماذون کی صورت میں تصرف کا حکم یعنی ملکیت خود غلام ہی کے لئے واقع ہوتی ہے حتی کہ اس کو اپنے اداء قرض اور نفقہ میں صرف کرنے کا اختیار ہوتا ہے اور باقی ماندہ میں آقا اس کا خلیفہ ہوتا ہے۔
The Book of the Permitted Slave... He said: And if he gave him permission for a specific thing, he is not a ma'dhun because it is using him for service. Its meaning is that he orders him to buy a garment for clothing or food as provision for his family. And this is because if he becomes ma'dhun, the door of using him for service would be closed, unlike when he says: 'Give me from the produce every month such and such' or 'Give me 1000 and you are free' because he asked for wealth from him, which cannot be obtained except by earning; or he says to him: 'Sit as a dyer or laundryman' because it is permission to buy what is necessary for both, and it is a type, so he becomes ma'dhun in all types. He said: And the acknowledgment of the ma'dhun of debts and usurpations is valid, and similarly of deposits, because acknowledgment is among the accessories of trade; for if it were not valid, people would avoid trading and dealing with him. There is no difference whether he owes a debt or not, provided the acknowledgment is during his health; and if it is during his illness, the health debt is given precedence, as with a free person, unlike the acknowledgment of wealth that is obligatory not due to trade, because he is like a restricted person in that respect.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is an intermadhhab dispute regarding the scope of permission for a ma'dhun slave. The Arabic matn first states a preliminary ruling: if the master gives permission for a specific item (like buying a specific garment), the slave is not ma'dhun because that is using him for personal service. However, if the master says 'give me monthly payment' or 'give me 1000 and you are free', the slave becomes ma'dhun because the master is demanding wealth which requires earning. Similarly, if the master instructs him to sit as a dyer or laundryman, it is effectively permission to purchase necessary items for that trade, making him ma'dhun in all types. The commentary then presents the core dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad (the three Hanafi imams) hold that if the master permits a specific type of trade, the ma'dhun becomes authorized in all types. Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i hold he is limited to that specific type. The same applies when the master prohibits a specific type. The opponents argue that idhn is like appointment as an agent (wikala) from the master, as the slave derives authority from him and ownership goes to the master, so the master can restrict. The Hanafi response is that idhn is actually lifting of restriction (fakk al-hajr) and dropping of the master's right, revealing the slave's ownership, so no restriction can be imposed. Unlike a wakeel who acts in another's property, the ma'dhun's ownership of the transaction and profit vests in himself; he can use it to pay his debts and expenses, and the master is simply his successor in the surplus. The commentary further clarifies that if the master forbids a specific type, the same disagreement applies.

Key Terms
مَأْذُون (ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade (usually general permission).
إذْن (idhn): Permission granted by the master to the slave to trade.
حَجْر (hajr): Legal restriction preventing a person from freely disposing of property.
وكيل (wakeel): An agent who acts on behalf of another (the principal) and does not own the subject matter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, what is the primary difference between a ma'dhun slave and a wakeel regarding the scope of authority?
A) The wakeel cannot be restricted to a specific trade type, while the ma'dhun can.
B) The ma'dhun owns the profit and can use it for his needs, whereas the wakeel does not own the subject matter.
C) Both are identical in all respects.
D) The wakeel's authority is absolute, while the ma'dhun's is limited.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, permission (idhn) for a trade type lifts all restrictions, making the ma'dhun authorized in all types—this is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.

The Valid Scope of a Ma'dhun's Acknowledgment (Iqrar)
Ruling on validity of ma'dhun's iqrar with trade-related scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a ma'dhun slave's acknowledgment (iqrar) is valid and distinguish between trade-related and non-trade obligations.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun's iqrar binds the master only for trade obligations; non-trade acknowledgments are invalid against the master.

This lesson examines the scope of a ma'dhun slave's acknowledgment (iqrar) of debts and obligations. The Hanafi school permits the ma'dhun to make binding acknowledgments only in matters connected to his trade permission. Why? Because the purpose of granting permission (idhn) is to facilitate trade, and people would not deal with him if his iqrar were unreliable. Let's explore the exact limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a ma'dhun slave's acknowledgment (iqrar) of a debt or obligation. The ruling: it is valid (sahih) but only for trade-related matters.
Non-trade acknowledgments are treated as if made by a restricted slave (mahjur).
2
Distinguish trade from non-trade obligations
Trade obligations include debts for goods purchased, deposits received, and prices of sold items. Non-trade obligations include guarantees (kafalah), usurpation (ghasb) of property, blood money (diyah) for injury, and mahr.
The passage gives examples: kafalah, ghasb, diyah, mahr are non-trade.
3
Note the condition of master's confirmation for wrongful acts
According to the three Imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani), acknowledgment of usurpation (ghasb) and destruction (itlaf) requires the master's confirmation to be binding on the master.
For trade debts, no confirmation is needed.
4
Apply the health versus illness distinction
If the ma'dhun acknowledges during a fatal illness (marad al-mawt), any health debts he owes take precedence over the acknowledged debt, just as with a free person.
This applies only to trade-related acknowledgments.
5
Understand the rationale (talil)
Iqrar is considered an accessory and necessity of trade. If people could not rely on his acknowledgment, they would avoid dealing with him, defeating the purpose of the trade permission.
This teleological explanation supports the validity of trade-related iqrar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله واقرار الماذون .... الخ ۔ اگر عبد مازون دیون و خوص کا اقرار کے تو اسکا را مجاز ہے خواہ قرضہ نفقد ہو یا کسی چیز کے دام ہوں۔ نیز آقا اس کی تصدیق کرے یا تکذیب ( ائمہ ثلاثہ کے نزدیک صرف دیون معاملہ کا اقرار جائز ہے اور غصب واتلاف کا اقرار اس صورت میں جائز ہے جب آقا اس کی تصدیق کرے ) اسی طرح ودائع اور امانات کا اقرار بھی جائز ہے اس لئے کہ اقرار تجارت کے توابع اور
لوازم میں سے ہے اگر اقرار صحیح نہ ہو تو لوگ اس کے ساتھ خرید و فروخت اور معاملہ کرنے سے پر ہیز کریں گے۔
قوله ولا فرق بين ما .... الخ ۔ پھر عبد ماذون کا اقرار بہر حال صحیح ہے خواہ مازون مذکور پر دین ہو گیا ہو یانہ ہوا ہو۔ بشرطیکہ اقرار اس کی حالت صحت میں واقع ہو اور اگر اس نے مرض الموت میں اقرار کیا تو اگر اس پر حالتِ صحت کا قرضہ ہو تو وہ اس اقرار پر مقدم کیا جائے گا۔ جیسے آزاد آدمی کی صورت میں ہوتا ہے۔ پھر یہ حکم اس اقرار میں ہے جو بسبب تجارت ہو۔ اگر اس نے ایسے مال کا اقرار کیا جو سبب تجارت کے علاوہ سے واجب ہوا ہے تو یہ اقرار صیح نہ ہوگا کیونکہ وہ ایسے اقرار کے حق میں عبد مجور کے مانند ہے ۔ مثلاً اس نے کفالت کا یا مال غیر کے اختلاف کا یا زخم کے جرمانہ کایا مہر کا اقرار کیا تو یہ آقا کے حق میں نافذ نہ ہوگا بلکہ وہ اس میں آزادی کے بعد ماخوذ ہوگا۔ جو امور ماذون کیلئے ناجائز ہیں
His statement: 'And the acknowledgment of the ma'dhun... etc.' – If the ma'dhun slave acknowledges debts or usurpations, his acknowledgment is valid, whether the debt is of a specific amount or the price of something. Also, whether the master confirms or denies it (according to the three Imams, only acknowledgment from trade debts is valid, and acknowledgment of usurpation and destruction is valid only if the master confirms it). Similarly, acknowledgment of deposits and trusts is valid, because acknowledgment is among the accessories and necessities of trade; if it were not valid, people would avoid buying and selling and dealing with him. His statement: 'And there is no difference between... etc.' – Then the acknowledgment of the ma'dhun slave is valid in any case, whether the said ma'dhun has a debt or not, provided the acknowledgment occurs during his health. If he acknowledges during his fatal illness, then if he has a health debt on him, it is given precedence over this acknowledgment, as in the case of a free person. Then this ruling applies to acknowledgment due to trade. If he acknowledges wealth that is obligatory from a cause other than trade, then this acknowledgment is not valid because he is like a restricted slave (mahjur) in that regard. For example, if he acknowledges a guarantee (kafalah), or the usurpation of another's property, or the blood money for a wound, or mahr, then it is not effective against the master; rather he will be held liable after freedom.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the validity of a ma'dhun slave's acknowledgment (iqrar) of debts and other obligations. The commentary expands on the matn's statement. First, it states that the ma'dhun's acknowledgment of debts and wrongful acts (ghasb) is valid, whether the master confirms or denies it. However, a parenthetical note mentions that according to the three Imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), only acknowledgment from trade debts is valid; for usurpation and destruction, it is valid only if the master confirms it. Then it adds that acknowledgment of deposits and trusts is also valid. The reason is that iqrar is an accessory to trade; if people could not rely on his acknowledgment, they would avoid dealing with him, harming trade. Next, it clarifies that it makes no difference whether the slave has prior debts or not, as long as the acknowledgment is made during his health. If made during terminal illness, health debts take precedence, just like for a free person. This entire ruling applies only to acknowledgments arising from trade. If the acknowledgment is for a non-trade obligation (e.g., kafalah, usurpation of another's property, diyah for injury, or mahr), it is not valid against the master; the slave will be liable after his manumission. This distinguishes the ma'dhun from a fully restricted slave (mahjur) for non-trade liabilities.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or confession of a right or debt owed by oneself.
غصوب (ghusub): Wrongful appropriation of property; usurpation.
ودائع (wada'i'): Deposits; items held in trust.
مرض الموت (marad al-mawt): Fatal illness; a legal condition affecting transactions, similar to death sickness in free persons.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following acknowledgments by a ma'dhun slave is NOT valid against his master?
A) Acknowledgment of a trade debt for purchased goods.
B) Acknowledgment of a deposit (wadi'ah) received.
C) Acknowledgment of a loan taken for personal use not related to trade.
D) Acknowledgment of the price of goods sold in trade.

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun's acknowledgment (iqrar) is binding on his master only for trade-related obligations; non-trade acknowledgments are invalid against the master.

Why the Master Cannot Restrict the Ma'dhun's Trade
Presents opponent's view and Hanafi proof on master's specification of trade type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi argument that a master cannot validly specify the trade type for a ma'dhun slave, using the distinction between ma'dhun and wakeel.

Core Idea
Permission forfeits restriction: the ma'dhun trades for himself, not for the master.

This passage addresses a key difference between the ma'dhun (permitted slave) and an agent (wakeel) or mudarib. The ability to restrict a trader depends on whose property is being transacted. Here, the opposing school argues that since the ma'dhun's authority derives from the master, the master can restrict him. The Hanafi school rebuts by showing that idhn (permission) fundamentally removes the master's right, leaving the slave free to trade in any lawful commerce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Positions
Read the passage carefully. The first part (prior to 'wa lana') states the opposing view: the master can specify trade type because the slave's authority comes from the master and ownership of profits goes to the master. The second part gives the Hanafi proof that this specification is invalid.
The passage begins mid-sentence; the opponent's argument is truncated.
2
Understand the Opposing Proof
The opponent analogizes the ma'dhun to a mudarib (investment partner). Just as an investor can restrict a mudarib's trade, the master can restrict the ma'dhun because both derive authority from the capital provider.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Proof
The Hanafi proof starts with 'wa lana' ('and for us'). It states that idhn is a removal of hajr (restriction) and a forfeiture of the master's right. Once the right is forfeited, the slave's ownership capacity appears fully, so no further restriction can be imposed.
The key concept: idhn is not merely a permission but a surrender of control.
4
Distinguish ma'dhun from Wakeel
The Hanafi response distinguishes the ma'dhun from a wakeel. A wakeel transacts in the master's property, so the master retains authority. But the ma'dhun transacts for himself; ownership of the subject matter goes to the slave. The master is only a successor (khalifah) in the residue after debts and maintenance.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: if the master specifies a trade type, the Hanafi view holds that the slave is not bound and may trade in any type. The opposing view would require the slave to adhere to the specification, and a violation might make the contract invalid.
This affects actual transactions: under Hanafi law, the slave's contracts are valid regardless of the master's restrictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المادون
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ۱۲
بنانا ہوتا ہے کیونکہ عبد ماذون کو ولایت تصرف آقاہی کی جانب سے حاصل ہوتی ہے اور حکم یعنی ملکیت آقا کو حاصل ہوتی ہے نہ کہ عبد مازون کر۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ آقا کو اس کے مجبور کرنے کا اختیار ہوتا ہے لہذا جس نوع تجارت کے ساتھ آقا نے تخصیص کی ہے وہ تخصیص صحیح ہوگی جیسے مضارب کے حق میں صحیح ہوتی ہے۔
قوله ولنا انه اسقاط .... الخ - ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ اذن فک حجر اور اسقاط حق ہے جس کی تحقیق شروع میں گذر چکی اور جب آقا نے اپنا حق ساقط کر دیا اور حجر دور ہو گیا تو غلام کی مالکیت ظاہر ہو گئی تو کسی خاص نوع تجارت کے ساتھ اختصاص نہ ہوگا بخلاف وکیل کے کہ وہ دوسرے کے مال میں تصرف کیا کرتا ہے اور اس کو ولایت تصرف دوسرے کی طرف سے حاصل ہوتی ہے اور ماذون کی صورت میں تصرف کا حکم یعنی ملکیت خود غلام ہی کے لئے واقع ہوتی ہے حتی کہ اس کو اپنے اداء قرض اور نفقہ میں صرف کرنے کا اختیار ہوتا ہے اور باقی ماندہ میں آقا اس کا خلیفہ ہوتا ہے۔
Book of the Ma'dhun. Ashraf al-Hidayah, Urdu commentary of Hidayah, volume 12. ... it is because the ma'dhun slave obtains the authority to transact from his master, and the effect, i.e., ownership, goes to the master, not to the ma'dhun slave. This is why the master has the right to restrict him. Therefore, the specification of a particular type of trade that the master has made is valid, just as it is valid in the case of a mudarib. He said: 'And for us, that it is forfeiture...' — our proof is that permission is a removal of restriction and a forfeiture of right, the verification of which has already passed at the beginning. When the master has forfeited his right and the restriction is removed, the slave's ownership becomes apparent, so there will be no restriction to a specific type of trade, contrary to the case of a wakeel, who transacts in another's property and derives authority from another. In the case of a ma'dhun, the effect of his transaction, i.e., ownership, accrues to the slave himself, such that he has the right to spend from it to pay his debts and maintenance, and the master is his successor in the remainder.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the validity of a master's specification of trade type for a ma'dhun (permitted) slave. The text first summarizes the opponent's position: that the master can validly restrict the slave to a specific type of trade, because the slave's authority to trade derives from the master, and the ownership of profits goes to the master. This is analogous to a mudarib, whom the investor can restrict. The passage then provides the Hanafi proof (from the commentator) using the phrase 'wa lana' ('and for us'). The Hanafi position is that permission (idhn) is fundamentally a removal of the master's right of restriction (hajr) and a forfeiture of that right. Once the master removes the restriction, the slave's own ownership capacity becomes manifest, and no restriction can be imposed. The wakeel (agent) is distinguished because he transacts in another's property (the master's), whereas the ma'dhun transacts for himself—the ownership of the transaction's subject matter goes to the slave, not the master. The master only has the role of a successor (khalifah) in any residue after the slave's debts and maintenance are paid. Therefore, according to the Hanafi school, any attempt by the master to confine the slave to a particular trade is invalid; the slave may trade in any type of lawful commerce.

Key Terms
عبد مأذون (‘abd ma’dhūn): A slave who is permitted by his master to engage in trade.
حجر (hajr): Legal restriction preventing the slave from transacting or being fully liable.
اذن (idhn): Permission granted by the master to the slave to engage in trade, which removes hajr and forfeits the master's right to restrict.
مضارب (muḍārib): A partner in a mudarabah contract who manages the capital.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi proof in this passage, why can the master not validly specify the trade type for a ma'dhun slave?
A) Because the slave is like a wakeel and must obey.
B) Because permission removes the master's right of restriction, making the slave's ownership capacity manifest.
C) Because the slave owns his own person and cannot be compelled.
D) Because the slave's trade is only for the master's benefit.

Key Takeaway
Once a master permits his slave to trade, he forfeits the right to restrict the type of trade; the slave contracts for himself, and the master is only a successor in the residue.

Rulings on Acknowledgment (Iqrar) by a Ma'dhun Slave
States the validity and limits of a ma'dhun's acknowledgment of debts and other obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a ma'dhun slave's acknowledgment is valid and distinguish between trade-related and non-trade acknowledgments.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun's acknowledgment is valid only for trade debts; non-trade acknowledgments require the master's approval.

In Hanafi fiqh, a ma'dhun slave has permission to trade, which grants limited legal capacity. One consequence is the ability to acknowledge debts arising from trade. However, not all acknowledgments are binding on the master. This lesson explores the precise limits based on the passage from Al-Hidayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Subject
Identify who a ma'dhun slave is and what iqrar (acknowledgment) means in legal terms.
Ma'dhun = slave with general permission to trade. Iqrar = unilateral admission of liability.
2
Determine the General Rule
Read the text to find the default ruling: a ma'dhun's acknowledgment of trade debts is valid regardless of the master's approval.
The text states: 'If a ma'dhun slave acknowledges debts... his acknowledgment is valid, whether the master confirms it or denies it.'
3
Examine the Exceptions
List the types of acknowledgments that are not automatically valid: non-trade obligations like usurpation, destruction, kafalah, mahr, blood money.
These require the master's confirmation to be binding on him.
4
Consider Temporal Conditions
Note the additional condition that the acknowledgment must be made while the slave is healthy; if made during terminal illness, earlier trade debts take precedence.
This mirrors the rule for free persons.
5
Understand the Rationale
Explain why the Hanafi school makes this distinction: acknowledgment is a necessary concomitant of trade; without it, people would not transact with the slave.
This is based on istihsan and practical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله واقرار الماذون .... الخ ۔ اگر عبد مازون دیون و خوص کا اقرار کے تو اسکا را مجاز ہے خواہ قرضہ نفقد ہو یا کسی چیز کے دام ہوں۔ نیز آقا اس کی تصدیق کرے یا تکذیب ( ائمہ ثلاثہ کے نزدیک صرف دیون معاملہ کا اقرار جائز ہے اور غصب واتلاف کا اقرار اس صورت میں جائز ہے جب آقا اس کی تصدیق کرے ) اسی طرح ودائع اور امانات کا اقرار بھی جائز ہے اس لئے کہ اقرار تجارت کے توابع اور لوازم میں سے ہے اگر اقرار صحیح نہ ہو تو لوگ اس کے ساتھ خرید و فروخت اور معاملہ کرنے سے پر ہیز کریں گے۔
قوله ولا فرق بين ما .... الخ ۔ پھر عبد ماذون کا اقرار بہر حال صحیح ہے خواہ مازون مذکور پر دین ہو گیا ہو یانہ ہوا ہو۔ بشرطیکہ اقرار اس کی حالت صحت میں واقع ہو اور اگر اس نے مرض الموت میں اقرار کیا تو اگر اس پر حالتِ صحت کا قرضہ ہو تو وہ اس اقرار پر مقدم کیا جائے گا۔ جیسے آزاد آدمی کی صورت میں ہوتا ہے۔ پھر یہ حکم اس اقرار میں ہے جو بسبب تجارت ہو۔ اگر اس نے ایسے مال کا اقرار کیا جو سبب تجارت کے علاوہ سے واجب ہوا ہے تو یہ اقرار صیح نہ ہوگا کیونکہ وہ ایسے اقرار کے حق میں عبد مجور کے مانند ہے ۔ مثلاً اس نے کفالت کا یا مال غیر کے اختلاف کا یا زخم کے جرمانہ کایا مہر کا اقرار کیا تو یہ آقا کے حق میں نافذ نہ ہوگا بلکہ وہ اس میں آزادی کے بعد ماخوذ ہوگا۔ جو امور ماذون کیلئے ناجائز ہیں
He said: 'And the acknowledgment of the ma'dhun...etc.' If a ma'dhun slave acknowledges debts and [specifics?], his acknowledgment is valid, whether the debt is a loss or the price of something. Also, whether the master confirms it or denies it. (According to the three Imams, only acknowledgment of transaction debts is valid; acknowledgment of usurpation and destruction is valid only if the master confirms it.) Similarly, acknowledgment of deposits and trusts is also valid, because acknowledgment is among the concomitants and necessities of trade. If acknowledgment were not valid, people would refrain from buying, selling, and transacting with him. He said: 'And there is no difference between what...etc.' Then the acknowledgment of a ma'dhun slave is valid in any case, whether or not he has become indebted to the extent of his value, provided the acknowledgment occurs while he is healthy. If he acknowledges during a terminal illness, and there is a debt from his healthy period, that debt takes precedence over this acknowledgment, just as in the case of a free man. Then this ruling applies to an acknowledgment that is due to trade. If he acknowledges something that becomes obligatory from a cause other than trade, this acknowledgment is not valid, because in that respect he is like a restricted slave (muhajjar). For example, if he acknowledges a guarantee (kafalah), or a dispute over another's property, or blood money for a wound, or mahr, this is not effective against the master; rather, he will be held liable after emancipation. These are matters that are impermissible for the ma'dhun.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage presents the rulings regarding acknowledgment (iqrar) by a ma'dhun slave. The text is from the commentary of Hidayah, explaining the base text's statement. First, it states that a ma'dhun's acknowledgment of debts related to trade is valid regardless of the master's approval—the master's confirmation or denial does not affect validity. This is because trade requires trust; if people could not rely on the slave's word, commerce would be stifled. However, if the acknowledgment concerns non-trade matters (e.g., usurpation, crimes, marriage dowry), the Hanafi school (Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad) restrict validity: only if the master confirms such an acknowledgment does it bind him. The text then adds that acknowledgment of deposits and trusts is also valid for the same reason. The second part clarifies that the acknowledgment is valid even if the slave has already incurred debts up to his value, provided he is healthy. If he acknowledges during a terminal illness, earlier trade debts take priority over new acknowledgments, as with a free person. Crucially, the ruling applies only to trade-related obligations. Acknowledgments arising from non-trade causes (like kafalah, damaged property, diyah, mahr) are not binding on the master; the slave will be personally liable after emancipation. This differentiates the ma'dhun from a fully restricted slave (muhajjar).

Key Terms
اقرار (iqrār): A legal acknowledgment by a person of a right or debt, creating liability.
غصب واتلاف (ghaṣb wa itlāf): Usurpation and destruction (of another's property), creating liability for compensation.
عبد محجور (‘abd maḥjūr): A slave under legal restriction (hajr), not permitted to trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, which type of acknowledgment by a ma'dhun slave is valid without the master's confirmation?
A) Acknowledgment of mahr from a marriage contract.
B) Acknowledgment of a debt from a sale transaction.
C) Acknowledgment of damages from a tort.
D) Acknowledgment of a guarantee (kafalah).

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun's acknowledgment binds only in trade matters; non-trade acknowledgments need the master's consent to take effect.

Ma'dhun Slave's Marriage and Marriage of His Slave
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ma'dhun's marriage authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a ma'dhun slave marrying himself and giving his female slave in marriage, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun cannot marry or give his slave in marriage because permission is for trade, not marriage.

The ma'dhun slave is a slave authorized by his master to trade, but does that permission extend to marriage contracts? This lesson unpacks a key intra-Hanafi dispute: whether the ma'dhun can marry himself or give his slaves in marriage. We will trace the positions of the founding imams, the underlying proofs, and how the ruling applies to other agents like fathers, guardians, and business partners. A note from Shaykh Sighnaqi will reveal an apparent contradiction in the Hidayah and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Central Act and Ruling
Start by identifying the act being discussed: a ma'dhun slave marrying himself or giving his male/female slave in marriage. The base ruling is that he cannot do any of these because marriage is not trade.
The ma'dhun's permission is for tijarah only.
2
State the Majority View and Its Proof
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the 'two sides'), the ma'dhun cannot give his female slave in marriage. Their proof: the permission (idhn) is limited to trade, and marriage is not a trade transaction, even if it yields dower. They require wealth acquisition to be through trade, not any means.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
3
State Abu Yusuf's View and His Proof
Imam Abu Yusuf holds that the ma'dhun can give his female slave in marriage because the dower (mahr) is wealth obtained through the contract, similar to hiring her out. Since the purpose of permission is wealth acquisition, this falls within it.
Abu Yusuf's qiyas: marriage of a slave is like ijarah (hiring).
4
Trace the Extension to Other Parties
The passage extends the same dispute to other agents: a minor ma'dhun (with guardian), mudarib, sharik 'inan, father, and guardian. According to the majority, none can give a female slave in marriage except the mukatab by consensus. Abu Yusuf permits it for all of them.
The mukatab is an exception; he has full authority over his female slave.
5
Analyze the Tanbih and Tarjih
Shaykh Sighnaqi points to an earlier passage in Hidayah (from Kitab al-Mukatab) that states the father and guardian are like a mukatab and can marry the minor's female slave. This seems to contradict the current passage. The Mabsut and other works support the earlier permissibility, leading to a tarjih that the father/guardian view from Mabsut is more authentic, possibly indicating two narrations.
This tanbih shows how later Hanafi scholarship resolves internal conflicts by checking multiple riwayat.

Source Text

تشریح قوله وليس ان يتزوج .... الخ عبد ماذون کو یہ اختیار نہیں ہے کہ وہ اپنا نکاح کرلے کیونکہ یہ کوئی تجارت نہیں ہے۔ نیز طرفین اور ائمہ ثلاثہ کے نزدیک عبد ماذون کے مال تجارت میں جو غلام یا باندی ہو ان کا نکاح بھی نہیں کراسکتا۔ ہاں امام ابو یوسف کے نزد یک باندی کی ترویج کا اختیار ہے کیونکہ باندی کے منافع سے مال یعنی مہر حاصل ہوگا اور اجازت سے مقصود تحصیل مال ہی ہے تو ایسا ہو گیا جیسے باندی کو مزدوری پر دے دینا کہ یہ جائز ہے۔ طرفین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اجازت کا مقصد بال کی تحصیل ہے یہ تو تسلیم ہے لیکن یہ مطلقا نہیں کہ وہ مکاتب کی طرح جیسے چاہے مال حاصل کرے بلکہ مال کی تحصیل ایسے طریقہ پر ہونی چاہیے کہ جو از باب تجارت ہو اور باندی کا نکاح کرنا کوئی تجارت نہیں ہے اس لئے وہ تزویج غلام کا مختار نہیں ہوتا۔ قوله وعلى هذا الخلاف ..... الخ -
